The Mastur Narrator: Definition and Ruling in Hanafi Usul
Classification of unacceptable narrators and ruling on mastur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of a mastur narrator and apply the correct Hanafi ruling regarding the acceptance of his report.

Core Idea
A mastur narrator's report is not accepted until his integrity is proven; he is treated like a fasiq in hadith.

In this passage, al-Pazdawi lists the types of narrators whose reports are not automatically accepted. He then focuses on the mastur – a narrator whose integrity is unknown. There is an internal Hanafi dispute: some consider him like a just person based on apparent integrity, but the correct view is that he is like a fasiq. This distinction is crucial for determining the validity of hadith transmissions and testimonies in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of narrators
The passage begins by naming seven categories of unacceptable narrators. List them: mastur, fasiq, sane child, insane, heedless, careless, follower of desire.
These are narrators whose reports are not automatically accepted; each has a specific reason for disqualification.
2
Define the mastur
The mastur is a narrator whose 'adalah (integrity) is unknown but whose apparent state is not sinful. Note the distinction between his outer and inner state.
He is outwardly practicing but inner integrity not verified.
3
Identify the two conflicting Hanafi views
One view (from Kitab al-Istihsan) equates the mastur with the fasiq; another (from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) treats him like a just person based on judging by apparent integrity.
The second view relies on qada' bi zahir al-adalah.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preferred view)
The author states that the correct view is what Imam Muhammad narrated: the mastur is like the fasiq, and his report is not a proof until his integrity is proven. This is undisputed in the chapter of hadith.
In hadith criticism, integrity is a strict condition.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if a narrator is mastur, his report about impurity of water (or any matter) is not accepted unless his integrity is verified through further investigation.
Example: a report about water purity from a mastur is not acted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الآخر: فأنواع أربعة: خبر المستور، وخبرُ الفاسق، وخبرُ الصبي العاقل، والمعتوه، والمغفّل، والمُساهل، وخبر صاحب الهوى. وأما خبر المستور فقد قال في كتاب الاستحسان: إنه مثل الفاسق فيما يُخبر عن نجاسة الماء. وفي رواية الحسن هو مثل العدل، وهذه الرواية بناء على القضاء بظاهر العدالة. والصحيح: ما حكاه محمد رحمه الله : أن المستور كالفاسق، لا يكون خبره حجةً حتى تظهر عدالته، وهذا بلا خلاف في باب الحديث؛
As for the other category: it consists of four types: the report of the mastur (one whose integrity is unknown), the report of the fasiq, the report of a sane child, the insane, the heedless, the careless, and the report of one who follows his desires. As for the report of the mastur, it is said in Kitab al-Istihsan that he is like the fasiq regarding what he reports about the impurity of water. And in the narration of al-Hasan, he is like the just person, and this narration is based on judging by apparent integrity. And the correct view is what Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated: that the mastur is like the fasiq; his report is not a proof until his integrity is manifest, and this is without dispute in the chapter of hadith.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage lists the categories of narrators whose reports are not automatically accepted: the mastur (unknown integrity), the fasiq (sinner), a sane minor (child who is intellectually capable), the insane, the heedless, the careless, and the one who follows his desires. The focus then turns to the mastur, defined as a narrator whose 'adalah (integrity) is not known but whose apparent state is not sinful. There is a disagreement within the Hanafi school: in the Kitab al-Istihsan, the mastur is equated with the fasiq, meaning his report is not accepted until his integrity is proven. In another riwaya from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, the mastur is considered like the 'adil (just) based on the principle that one judges by apparent integrity. However, the author states that the correct view (sahih) is what Imam Muhammad narrated: the mastur is like the fasiq, and his report is not hujja until his 'adalah is established. This ruling is undisputed in the chapter of hadith, meaning that in hadith criticism, a mastur narrator is not acceptable unless his integrity is verified.

Key Terms
المستور (al-mastūr): A narrator whose integrity ('adalah) is unknown; his apparent state is not sinful but his inner state is not verified.
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, whose testimony/report is rejected.
رواية الحسن (riwāyat al-Ḥasan): A transmission from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, a Hanafi scholar, who narrated a different view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi school, how is the report of a mastur narrator treated?
A) It is accepted like the report of an 'adil narrator.
B) It is rejected outright like the report of a fasiq.
C) It is like the report of a fasiq and is not accepted until his integrity is shown.
D) It is accepted only if he is a child.

Key Takeaway
The report of a mastur narrator is not a proof (hujja) until his integrity is established; in hadith, he is treated like a fasiq.

Hanafi Rules on Accepting Reports from Narrators
Classifying narrators by reliability and ruling on their reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on accepting reports from al-mastur, al-fasiq, and non-adult narrators, and apply the principles to new scenarios.

Core Idea
A report is only a proof if the narrator is reliable; the mastur and fasiq fail this test until justice is proven.

In Hanafi usul, the acceptance of a report hinges on the narrator's character (adala). This lesson examines four categories of narrators whose reports are generally not accepted as legal proofs: the mastur (unknown justice), fasiq (sinner), discerning child, and insane person. We will explore the rulings, the rationale, and the limited exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Narrator Categories
List the four types of narrators discussed: mastur, fasiq, sane child, and insane. Note that the passage also mentions the negligent (mughaffal) and careless (musahil).
These categories share the feature that their reports are not binding proofs.
2
State the Ruling for Each
For mastur: like fasiq until justice is proven. For fasiq: not a proof in religion. For child and insane: like a disbeliever; their report is not a proof. Memorize these as axioms.
The ruling for mastur has a dispute; the correct view is as above.
3
Recognize the Exception for Fasiq in Personal Matters
When a fasiq reports on halal/haram in specific personal matters (e.g., food), the listener may use his judgment (taharrī) due to necessity. This does not apply to general religious rulings.
Contrast with gifts and agencies where necessity is binding.
4
Analyze the Hadith Example
The hadith about orphans' wealth is cited to show Hanafi methodology: they examine chains and deem the report mawquf or mursal, thus not a proof. This illustrates how they handle reports from weak narrators.
Notice that the opponent's reliance on apparent discontinuity is rejected.
5
Understand the Dispute about Mastur
Some hold that mastur is like adil due to apparent justice. The Hanafi response: in hadith matters there is consensus that mastur is like fasiq, and the correct view requires verification.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi view is mufta bihi.

Source Text

فهذا انقطاع باطن معنوي أعرض عنه الخصم، وتمسك بظاهر الانقطاع، كما هو دأبه.
وأما القسم الآخر : فأنواع أربعة:
خبر المستور، وخبرُ الفاسق، وخبرُ الصبي العاقل، والمعتوه، والمغفّل، والمُساهل، وخبر صاحب الهوى.
وأما خبر المستور فقد قال في كتاب الاستحسان: إنه مثل الفاسق فيما يُخبر عن نجاسة الماء.
وفي رواية الحسن هو مثل العدل، وهذه الرواية بناء على القضاء بظاهر العدالة.
والصحيح: ما حكاه محمد رحمه الله : أن المستور كالفاسق، لا يكون خبره حجةً حتى تظهر عدالته، وهذا بلا خلاف في باب الحديث؛
وقد روى حديث "ابتغوا في أموال اليتامى خيراً؛ كي لا تأكلها الزكاة"، ورواه الدارقطني من طريق مندل بن علي، وهو ضعيف، ومن طريق العرزمي، وهو ضعيف، ورواه ابن عدي من طريق الإفريقي، وهو ضعيف. وقال الدارقطني في العلل : رواه حسين المعلّم عن مكحول عن عمرو بن شعيب عن ابن المسيب عن عمر ورواه ابن عيينة عن عمرو بن دينار عن عمرو بن شعيب عن عمر، ولم يذكر ابن المسيب، وهذا أصح. قلت : فآل الأمر إلى أنه الموقوف الذي أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، ولم يبق في الباب مرفوع إلا مرسل ابن ماهَك والمرسل ليس بحجة عندهم، والله أعلم.
وأما خبر الفاسق فليس بحجة في الدين أصلاً؛ لرجحان كذبه على صدقه.
وقد قال محمد رحمه الله في الفاسق إذا أخبر بحل أو حُرمة : إن السامع يُحكّم رأيه فيه؛ لأن ذلك أمر خاص لا يستقيم طلبه وتلقيه من جهة العدول، فوجب التحري في خبره.
فأما ها هنا فلا ضرورة في المصير إلى روايته، وفي العدول كثرة وغنية، إلا أن الضرورة في حل الطعام والشراب غير لازمة؛ لأن العمل بالأصل ممكن، وهو أن الماء طاهر في الأصل، فلم يجعل الفسق هَدَراً.
بخلاف خبر الفاسق في الهدايا والوكالات ونحوها؛ لأن الضرورة ثَمَّة لازمة، وفيه وجه آخر نذكره في باب محل الخبر إن شاء الله تعالى.
وأما الصبي، والمعتوه: فقد ذكر محمد رحمه الله في كتاب الاستحسان بعد ذكر العدل والفاسق والكافر وكذلك الصبي والمعتوه إذا عقلا ما يقولان فقال بعضُهم : هما مثل العدل المسلم البالغ.
والصحيح أنهما مثل الكافر، لا تقوم الحجة بخبرهما، ولا يُفوَّض أمر الدين إليهما ؛ لما قلنا إن خبرهما لا يصلح ملزماً بحال؛ لأن الولاية المتعدية : فرع للولاية القائمة، وليس لهما ولاية ملزمة في حق أنفسهما، وإنما هي مجوزة، فكيف تثبت متعدية ملزمة؟!
So this is a hidden, meaningful discontinuity that the opponent ignored, and he clung to the apparent discontinuity, as is his habit. As for the other category: it has four types: the report of the mastur (one whose justice is unknown), the report of the fasiq (sinner), the report of the sane child, the insane, the negligent, the careless, and the report of one who follows his desire. As for the report of the mastur, it was stated in Kitab al-Istihsan: he is like the fasiq regarding what he reports about the impurity of water. And in the riwaya of Hasan, he is like the adil (just), and this riwaya is based on judging by apparent justice. The sahih (correct) is what Muhammad رحمه الله narrated: that the mastur is like the fasiq, his report is not a proof until his justice becomes apparent, and this is without dispute in the chapter of hadith. And he narrated the hadith: "Seek goodness in the wealth of orphans so that zakat does not consume it." Al-Daraqutni narrated it through the route of Mandal ibn Ali, who is weak, and through the route of al-Arzami, who is weak, and Ibn Adi narrated it through the route of al-Ifriqi, who is weak. Al-Daraqutni said in al-Ilal: Husayn al-Mu'allim narrated it from Makhul from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from Ibn al-Musayyib from Umar, and Ibn Uyayna narrated it from Amr ibn Dinar from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from Umar, and he did not mention Ibn al-Musayyib, and this is more correct. I say: the matter finally becomes that it is the mawquf (stopped) report that Ibn Abi Shayba recorded, and there remains no marfu' (raised) report except the mursal of Ibn Mahak, and the mursal is not a proof according to them, and Allah knows best. As for the report of the fasiq, it is not a proof in religion at all, because his lying outweighs his truthfulness. Muhammad رحمه الله said about the fasiq if he reports about something being halal or haram: the listener uses his own judgment regarding it, because that is a specific matter that cannot be properly sought and received from the just, so it is necessary to investigate his report. So here there is no necessity to resort to his narration, and there are plenty of just people sufficient, except that necessity in resolving food and drink is not binding, because acting on the original principle is possible, which is that water is originally pure, so the sinfulness is not disregarded. Contrary to the report of the fasiq in matters of gifts, agencies, and the like, because there the necessity is binding. And there is another aspect we will mention in the chapter on the place of the report, if Allah wills. As for the child and the insane: Muhammad رحمه الله mentioned in Kitab al-Istihsan after mentioning the just, the sinner, and the disbeliever, and likewise the child and the insane if they understand what they say: some said they are like the just adult Muslim. And the correct is that they are like the disbeliever; their report does not constitute a proof, and the matter of religion is not entrusted to them, because we said their report is not binding at all, since transitive authority is a branch of established authority, and they have no binding authority over themselves; it is only permissive, so how can transitive binding authority be established?!
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the conditions for accepting reports from various categories of narrators. The author first distinguishes between a hidden meaningful discontinuity (in the chain or text) and apparent discontinuity, noting that the opponent (likely the Shafi'i school) wrongly clung to the latter. The main focus is on reports of the mastur (whose justice is unknown), fasiq, child, and insane. For the mastur, the correct position (sahih) is that he is like the fasiq: his report is not a proof until his justice is verified, except in hadith matters where there is consensus. The author cites a hadith about orphans' wealth and analyzes its chains, concluding it is mawquf or mursal, thus not a proof. For the fasiq, his report is not a proof in religion due to the predominance of falsehood. However, in matters of halal and haram specific to an individual (like food), the listener may exercise personal judgment (taharrī) because of necessity, but this does not apply to general religious rulings. For the child and insane, they are like disbelievers; their reports are not binding because they lack authority over themselves, so they cannot have transitive authority.

Key Terms
المستور (al-mastur): A narrator whose condition of justice ('adala) is unknown; not known to be sinful but not proven just.
الفاسق (al-fasiq): A narrator who is sinful, i.e., commits major sins or persists on minor sins.
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-'aqil): A child who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz) and understands what he says, but is not yet baligh.
المعتوه (al-ma'tuh): A person afflicted with insanity or severe mental deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are like a just Muslim adult; their report is a proof.
B) They are like a disbeliever; their report is not a proof.
C) They are like a fasiq; their report is investigated case by case.
D) They are like a mastur; their report is accepted after apparent justice.

Key Takeaway
Reports from mastur, fasiq, child, and insane narrators are not binding proofs in Hanafi usul; only reports from reliable, just adults (adil) are accepted as legal evidence.

The Child's Report (Riwayat al-Sabi) is Not Binding: Hanafi Usul
Stating the rule for a child's report by analogy to a disbeliever

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a child's report and identify the qiyas (analogy) used to derive it, comparing it to the report of a disbeliever and a fasiq.

Core Idea
A child's report is not binding because the child is not legally responsible (mukallaf), analogous to a disbeliever.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul establishes a key principle in Hanafi usul: the report (riwayah) of a child (sabi) carries no binding legal authority. The author uses a clear analogy (qiyas) to the report of a disbeliever, arguing that both are from those not addressed by the Law (ghayr mukhatab). Importantly, this is distinguished from the report of a fasiq Muslim, which may be accepted with precaution (ihtiyat). Understanding this hierarchy helps us see how the Hanafi school evaluates the reliability of reports based on the epistemic status of the reporter.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the explicit ruling on the child's report: it is not binding (la talzim).
The text says: 'رواية الصبي فيه : يجب أن تكون مثل رواية الكافر' — the child's narration is like the disbeliever's.
2
Identify the Asl (Original Case)
Find the case that serves as the basis for the analogy: the report of a disbeliever (kafir) is not binding.
The passage states: 'بمنزلة خبر الكافر' — like the report of a disbeliever.
3
Identify the Far' (New Case)
The new case under consideration is the report of a child.
The entire passage revolves around riwayat al-sabi.
4
Identify the Illa (Effective Cause)
Determine the common characteristic that justifies the analogy: both the child and the disbeliever are not legally responsible (ghayr mukhatab).
The text explicitly says: 'لأنه غير مخاطب' — because he is not addressed.
5
Compare with the Fasiq
Contrast the child's case with that of a fasiq Muslim, whose report is treated with caution (ihtiyat).
The fasiq is mukallaf, so his report may be accepted in contexts where caution is recommended.

And the dhimmi: it is not binding on him because he is not addressed [by the Law]. So another person becomes intended by his report, and it becomes from the category of coercion, like the report of a disbeliever. Contrary to the slave, due to what we said. And the insane person is like the child; Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) explicitly stated that in a place of al-Mabsut: 'Do you not see that the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) bore [hadith] in their childhood and transmitted in their adulthood?' (1) His statement: 'Do you not see that the Companions bore [hadith] in their childhood and transmitted in their adulthood?' For example, Ibn Abbas, Ibn al-Zubayr, and al-Nu'man ibn Bashir. As for Abdullah ibn Abbas: al-Mizzi said in al-Tahdhib: It is narrated from him that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) died when I was thirteen years old.' And Abu Ishaq al-Sabi'i said from Sa'id ibn Jubayr from Ibn Abbas: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) died when I was fifteen years old.' Ahmad ibn Hanbal said: This is correct. Similarly, the authors on the Companions mentioned it, like Abu Nu'aym, Ibn Manda, Ibn Abd al-Barr, and others. Among what Ibn Abbas learned in his childhood: what al-Bukhari narrated from Abd al-Rahman ibn Abis: I heard Ibn Abbas, and he was asked: 'Did you attend the Eid with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)?' He prayed two rak'ahs, then gave a sermon, then he came to the women and admonished them, and they began to throw their jewelry into Bilal's garment as charity.' The hadith. And what Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, and al-Daraqutni narrated from him: He said: 'I rode behind [the Prophet]', and in another wording: 'I was riding behind the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he said: O boy, shall I teach you words by which Allah will benefit you: Guard Allah, He will guard you; Guard Allah, you will find Him before you.' The hadith. As for Ibn al-Zubayr: The historians and authorities agree that he was the first child born in Islam in Medina from the Quraysh, and that he was born in the second year. Similarly, those who wrote about the Companions. Among what Ibn al-Zubayr memorized in his childhood: what al-Bukhari narrated from him: He said: 'On the day of the Trench, I and Umar ibn Abi Salama were in the fortress where the wives of the Prophet (peace be upon him) were. He would lift me up, and I would lift him. When he lifted me, I saw my father as he passed by to Banu Qurayza, and he was fighting with the Prophet (peace be upon him). The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: Who will go to Banu Qurayza? So al-Zubayr went. When he returned, I said: O father! I saw you passing to Banu Qurayza. In another wording: I mentioned that to my father, and he said: Did you see me, my son? I said: Yes. He said: By Allah, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) combined his parents for me that day, saying: May my father and mother be your ransom. And the Trench was in the fourth or fifth year, so his age was four years and some months, and Allah knows best. As for al-Nu'man ibn Bashir: He was the first child born to the Ansar after the Hijra. al-Waqidi said: He was born at the head of fourteen months after the Hijra. He said in al-Tahdhib: Ibn Ma'in said: The people of Medina say: He did not hear from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the people of Iraq consider his hearing from him to be authentic. He does not say 'I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)' except in the hadith of al-Sha'bi: 'Indeed in the body there is a piece of flesh' - and the rest he says 'from'. This hadith in which he explicitly states hearing is agreed upon, and Allah knows best. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said concerning the disbeliever who reports the impurity of water: His report is not acted upon, and he should perform wudu with it. If he performs tayammum and pours out the water, that is more beloved to me. And concerning the fasiq, he made precaution the principle, and it must be the same in the narration of hadith where precaution is recommended. And similarly, the narration of the child in this matter: it must be like the narration of the disbeliever, not like the Muslim fasiq.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul addresses the admissibility of reports (riwāyah) from a child (sabī) in legal matters. The author compares the child's report to that of a disbeliever (kāfir), arguing that since the child is not addressed by the law (mukhaṭab), his report is not binding and is treated as coercion (ilzām), unlike the report of a slave or a Muslim fāsiq. The insane person (ma‘tūh) is equated with the child. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybānī explicitly states this in al-Mabsūt, supported by the fact that the Companions (ṣaḥābah) narrated from the Prophet while young and transmitted later in adulthood. The passage then provides extensive biographical examples (Ibn ‘Abbās, Ibn al-Zubayr, al-Nu‘mān ibn Bashīr) to show that childhood narration is possible and accepted when the narrator later becomes reliable, but the report of a child who is not yet legally responsible is not accepted. Returning to the main argument, the author reports Muhammad's ruling on a disbeliever's news about water impurity: it is not accepted, and one should use the water and only resort to tayammum as a precaution. For a fāsiq, the principle is caution (iḥtiyāṭ), and this applies to hadith narration where caution is recommended. Consequently, the child's narration in this matter is treated like that of a disbeliever, not like a fāsiq Muslim. This establishes a clear hierarchy in Hanafi usul: the reports of those not mukallaf (child, insane, disbeliever) are not binding, while a fāsiq's report may be accepted with caution.

Key Terms
رواية الصبي (riwāyat al-ṣabī): The transmission of a report (hadith or testimony) by a minor before reaching puberty.
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A Muslim who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, affecting his reliability.
الاحتياط (al-iḥtiyāṭ): Legal precaution, a principle applied when there is doubt, requiring extra care in accepting reports or performing acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the Hanafi school treat the riwāyah (report) of a child (sabī) in matters of impurity and hadith?
A) Like the report of a fāsiq Muslim, requiring caution.
B) Like the report of a disbeliever (kāfir), not accepted as binding.
C) Like the report of a slave, subject to verification.
D) Fully accepted because Companions narrated in childhood.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the report of a child is not binding because the child is not legally responsible; it is analogous to the disbeliever's report, unlike the fasiq's report which is treated with caution.

Ruling on Accepting Narrations from Innovators (Mubtadi')
Ruling on rejecting narrations from those who invite to bid'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the acceptance of narrations from innovators who invite to bid'ah, and explain the talil (reasoning) that distinguishes between hadith narration and legal testimony.

Core Idea
The narration of a caller to bid'ah is rejected due to the motive to fabricate; his testimony is accepted as no such motive exists.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi articulates a key principle in Hanafi hadith criticism: a narrator who actively promotes innovation (bid'ah) is not reliable for transmitting the Prophet's sunnah. However, the same individual's testimony in court is not automatically rejected. The reasoning (talil) is based on the presence or absence of a motive to lie.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi position: the narration of one who adopts bid'ah and calls people to it is not accepted in the chapter of sunan (hadith).
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The passage gives the reason: because disputation and calling to desire cause a motive to fabricate hadith. The narrator is not considered trustworthy concerning the Prophet's words.
The key is the element of da'wah (invitation) which increases the motive.
3
Distinguish from legal testimony
In contrast, the same person's testimony in legal cases (shahadah) is not rejected because the motive to forge testimony is not present in that context.
This shows that the ruling is specific to hadith narration, not a blanket rejection.
4
Draw the analogy with fasiq
The passage concludes that such a narrator is treated like a fasiq (sinner) in hadith matters.
This means the same rules of jarh wa ta'dil apply: such a narrator is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك من قال بالإلهام إنه حجةٌ: يجب أن لا تجوز شهادته أيضاً. وأما في باب السنن : فإن المذهب المختار عندنا أن لا تُقبل روايةُ مَن انتحل الهوى والبدعة، ودعا الناس إليه. على هذا أئمةُ الفقه والحديث كلُّهم؛ لأن المحاجة والدعوة إلى الهوى سبب داع إلى التقول فلا يُؤتمن على حديث رسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام. وليس كذلك الشهادة في حقوق الناس ؛ لأن ذلك لا يدعو إلى التزوير في ذلك الباب، فلم تُرَدَّ شهادته. فإذا صح هذا كان صاحب الهوى بمنزلة الفاسق في باب السنن والأحاديث، والله أعلم.
And likewise, whoever says that inspiration is a proof: his testimony must also not be accepted. As for the chapter of narrations (sunan), the chosen school with us is that the narration of one who adopts desires and innovation and calls people to it is not accepted. All the imams of fiqh and hadith are upon this; because disputation and calling to desire is a cause that invites false attribution, so he is not trustworthy concerning the hadith of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. And it is not the same for testimony in the rights of people; because that does not invite forgery in that chapter, so his testimony is not rejected. So if this is established, the person of desire is like a sinner (fasiq) in the chapter of sunan and hadiths. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi position (and that of all leading scholars of fiqh and hadith) that a narrator who openly adopts and propagates bid'ah (innovation) is not acceptable in hadith narration. The reasoning is that such a person's engagement in disputation and invitation to his innovation creates a motive to fabricate hadith to support his position. In contrast, such a narrator's testimony in legal cases concerning the rights of people is not rejected, because the motive to forge testimony is not present in that context. Thus, in the realm of hadith, the innovator is treated like a fasiq (sinner) whose narration is rejected.

Key Terms
بدعة (bid'ah): Innovation in religion that one adopts and calls to.
فاسق (fasiq): One who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; here used to describe the innovator in hadith context.
هوى (hawa): Personal desire leading to deviation.
شهادة (shahadah): Legal testimony in court regarding rights of people.
رواية (riwayah): Narration of hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the testimony of a person who invites to bid'ah accepted in legal cases?
A) Because the person is not a fasiq in that context.
B) Because there is no motive to forge testimony.
C) Because all scholars agree on this.
D) Because testimony is more important than hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects the narration of a caller to bid'ah due to the motive to fabricate, but accepts his legal testimony, as the motive is absent.

Five Categories for Khabar al-Wahid and Dispute on Punishments
Classification of khabar al-wahid's authority and intra-Hanafi dispute on punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories where khabar al-wahid is authoritative and explain the Hanafi dispute on its application to hudud punishments.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid is proof in most matters, but punishments are disputed due to the principle of averting by doubts.

This passage from Bazdawi's Usul classifies the matters where a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) can serve as legal proof. It outlines five categories and then zooms into a key intra-Hanafi dispute: can punishments be established by a khabar al-wahid? Understanding this classification is essential for grasping the scope of hadith-based rulings in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five categories
Identify and memorize the five types: pure divine rights (non-punishment), divine punishments, pure obligations of servants, non-obligation rights, mixed obligation rights. These are the areas where khabar al-wahid may be invoked.
The first category (worship) is undisputed; the second (punishments) is the focus of ikhtilaf.
2
Locate the consensus category
Recognize that for the first category (non-punishment divine rights), there is no dispute: khabar al-wahid is accepted. This is axiomatic in the Hanafi school.
3
Identify the disputed category
The second category—punishments (hudud)—is where the ikhtilaf occurs. Two opinions are presented within the Hanafi school.
4
Extract the two positions
State Abu Yusuf's position (permissible, supported by al-Jassas) and al-Karkhi's position (not permissible). Note the proofs each side gives.
Abu Yusuf uses qiyas to bayyinat and dalalat al-nass; al-Karkhi uses the principle of shubha.
5
Analyze the proofs
Compare the two analogies: Abu Yusuf argues that khabar al-wahid provides sufficient knowledge for action, like evidence in court; al-Karkhi argues that it carries doubt, and punishments are averted by doubts, similar to qiyas.
This step deepens understanding of usul reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان محل الخبر وهو الذي جعل الخبر فيه حجةً، وذلك خمسة أنواع : ما يخلص الله تعالى حقاً من شرائعه مما ليس بعقوبة. والثاني : ما هو عقوبة من حقوقه. والثالث : من حقوق العباد ما فيه إلزام محض. والرابع من حقوق العباد ما ليس فيه إلزام. والخامس : من حقوق العباد ما كان فيه إلزام من وجه، دون وجه. وأما الأول : فمثل عامة شرائع العبادات وما شاكلها، وخبر الواحد فيها حجة، على ما قلنا من شرائطه. وأما القسم الثاني : فإن أبا يوسف رحمه الله قال فيما روي عنه: إنه يجوز إثبات العقوبات بالآحاد وهو اختيار الجصاص رحمه الله. واختيار الكرخي رحمه الله : أنه لا يجوز ذلك. ووجه القول الأول : أن خبر الواحد يفيد من العلم ما يصلح العمل به في إقامة الحدود كما في البينات في مجالس الحكم، وكما يجوز إثباتها بدلالة النص. ووجه القول الآخر: أن إثبات الحدود بالشبهات لا يجوز، فإذا تمكنت في الدليل شبهة : لم يجز إثباتها، كما لم يجز إثباتها بالقياس.
Chapter: Clarifying the context of the report. It is that which makes the report a proof. That is five types: First: what belongs exclusively to Allah Almighty as a right from His laws that is not a punishment. Second: what is a punishment from His rights. Third: from the rights of the servants, what involves pure obligation. Fourth: from the rights of the servants, what does not involve obligation. Fifth: from the rights of the servants, what involves obligation from one aspect but not from another. As for the first type: it is like the general laws of worship and the like, and the solitary report is a proof in it, according to the conditions we have mentioned. As for the second type: Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said in what is narrated from him: it is permissible to establish punishments through solitary reports, and that is the choice of al-Jassas, may Allah have mercy on him. And the choice of al-Karkhi, may Allah have mercy on him, is that it is not permissible. The proof of the first view: that the solitary report provides such knowledge that it is suitable for acting upon it in establishing punishments, just as in evidence (bayyinat) in courts of judgment, and just as it is permissible to establish them through textual indication (dalalat al-nass). And the proof of the other view: that establishing punishments on the basis of doubts (shubuhat) is not permissible, so if a doubt arises in the evidence, it is not permissible to establish them, just as it is not permissible to establish them through analogy (qiyas).
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents the chapter on 'the context of the report' (mahall al-khabar), i.e., the types of matters in which khabar al-wahid (solitary report) is accepted as a proof. The text enumerates five categories: (1) purely divine rights that are not punishments (e.g., acts of worship); (2) divine rights that are punishments (hudud); (3) pure obligations owed to people; (4) matters of people's rights that do not involve obligation; (5) matters that involve obligation from one perspective but not another. For the first category, khabar al-wahid is accepted by consensus. For the second category—punishments—there is a dispute between the Hanafi scholars Abu Yusuf (and al-Jassas) who allow establishing punishments via ahad reports, and al-Karkhi who does not. Abu Yusuf's proof relies on analogy: just as punishments are established by evidence (bayyinat) in court and by dalalat al-nass (textual implication), so too can they be established by khabar al-wahid, since it provides sufficient knowledge for action. Al-Karkhi's proof argues that punishments are averted by doubts (shubuhat), and khabar al-wahid carries an element of doubt (since it is not mutawatir), so it cannot be used to establish punishments, just as qiyas is not acceptable in such matters.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A solitary report that is accepted as proof in certain categories of rulings.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment for crimes, considered a divine right.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt that prevents the application of punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Karkhi, what is the ruling on establishing hudud punishments through khabar al-wahid?
A) Permissible
B) Not permissible
C) Permissible only if there are multiple narrators
D) Permissible only for non-hudud punishments

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid is authoritative in all matters except where doubt prevents its application, such as punishments according to some Hanafis.

The Five Categories of Legal Matters for Khabar al-Wahid
Classifies five types of legal matters for accepting solitary reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five categories of legal matters in which khabar al-wahid is accepted, and apply the correct conditions for each category.

Core Idea
The authority of khabar al-wahid varies by legal matter: hudud require caution, pure obligations demand strict testimony, while non-obligatory rights accept even a child's report.

Al-Bazdawi presents a systematic classification that determines how and when a solitary report can be used as legal evidence. By understanding these categories, you will see why Hanafi usul treats different types of cases differently, showing the school's nuanced approach to probative value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
List the five types as given: (1) purely divine non-punishment, (2) divine punishment, (3) servant rights with pure obligation, (4) servant rights without obligation, (5) mixed obligation.
Memorize the Arabic labels: ما يخلص الله تعالى حقاً من شرائعه مما ليس بعقوبة, ما هو عقوبة من حقوقه, etc.
2
Determine the rule for each category
For category 1, khabar al-wahid is accepted with standard conditions. For category 2, there is ikhtilaf (Abu Yusuf permits, al-Karkhi prohibits). For category 3, strict conditions (shahada, number, etc.) are required. For category 4, only tamyiz (discrimination) is needed. For category 5, the text does not elaborate, but it would be intermediate.
Pay attention to the special conditions for categories 3 and 4.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on hudud
Examine Abu Yusuf's proof (khabar al-wahid suffices for action, like bayyina) and al-Karkhi's proof (hudud are averted by shubuhat, and khabar al-wahid involves doubt). Understand how each side uses analogy and principles.
The passage notes that Abu Hanifa himself did not use a gharib report for liwat, but this is not a definitive rejection.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Given a hypothetical case, decide which category it falls into and what conditions apply for khabar al-wahid. For instance, testimony about the crescent of Eid falls under category 3 (pure obligation).
Practice with the examples given in the passage: wakala, mudaraba, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

* أما الأول : فمثل عامة شرائع العبادات وما شاكلها، وخبر الواحد فيها حجة، على ما قلنا من شرائطه.

فأما البينة : فإنما صارت حجةً بالنص الذي لا شبهة فيه.

يُرى أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله لم يوجب الحدَّ في اللواطة بالقياس، ولا بالخبر الغريب من الآحاد.
وأما القسم الثالث : فلا يثبت إلا بلفظ الشهادة، والعدد عند الإمكان، وقيام الأهلية بالولاية مع سائر شرائط الأخبار؛ لما فيها من محض الإلزام، وتوكيداً لها؛ لما يُخاف فيها من وجوه التزوير والتلبيس؛ صيانةً للحقوق المعصومة، وذلك مما يطول ذكره.

العدالة، وذلك مثل الوكالات، والمضاربات والرسالات في الهدايا والإذن في التجارات، وما أشبه ذلك، فيُقبل فيها خبر الصبي والكافر. ولهذا قلنا في الفاسق إذا أخبر رجلاً أن فلاناً وكلك بكذا، فوقع في قلبه صدقه : حلَّ له العمل به، وذلك لوجهين:
Chapter: Explanation of the Place of the Report. It is that in which the report is made a proof, and that is of five types: First, what is purely Allah's right from His laws that is not a punishment. Second, what is a punishment from His rights. Third, from the rights of servants is that which contains pure obligation. Fourth, from the rights of servants is that which contains no obligation. Fifth, from the rights of servants is that which contains obligation from one aspect and not from another. As for the first: it is like the general laws of worship and what resembles them, and the solitary report is a proof therein, according to what we have said of its conditions. As for the second section: Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said in what is related from him: it is permissible to establish punishments by solitary reports, and this is the choice of al-Jassas, may Allah have mercy on him. The choice of al-Karkhi, may Allah have mercy on him, is that it is not permissible. The proof of the first statement: that the solitary report conveys from knowledge what suffices for acting upon it in establishing hadd penalties, just as it does in the case of evidence (bayyināt) in courts of judgment, and just as it is permissible to establish them by textual indication. The proof of the other statement: that establishing hadd penalties by doubtful matters is not permissible, so if a doubt occurs in the proof, it is not permissible to establish them, just as it is not permissible to establish them by analogy. As for the evidence (bayyina): it is made a proof only by a text that has no doubt in it. Allah Most High said: 'And call to witness from among you four witnesses' (Quran 4:15). Do you not see that Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, did not make obligatory the hadd penalty for sodomy by analogy, nor by a strange solitary report. As for the third section: it is not established except by the word of testimony, and the number when possible, and the competence of guardianship along with the other conditions of reports, because of the pure obligation contained therein, and as an emphasis for it, due to the fear of forms of forgery and deception; to safeguard protected rights, and that which would be lengthy to mention. The testimony concerning the crescent of breaking the fast is of this section. As for the fourth section: it is established by solitary reports on condition of discrimination, without uprightness, such as agency, partnerships, sending gifts, permission for transactions, and the like. Thus, the report of a child or an unbeliever is accepted therein. For this reason, we say concerning a corrupt person (fāsiq) if he informs a man that so-and-so appointed you as agent for such-and-such, and it settles in his heart that he is truthful: it is permissible for him to act upon it, and that for two reasons:
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a classification from Usul al-Bazdawi explaining the five categories of legal matters in which a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) can be accepted as evidence. The author first lists the five types: (1) purely divine rights that are not punishments (e.g., worship acts), (2) divine punishments (hudud), (3) servant rights involving pure obligation, (4) servant rights with no obligation, and (5) servant rights with obligation from one aspect but not another. For each type, he explains the conditions for accepting solitary reports.

For the first type (e.g., prayers, fasting, zakat), solitary reports are accepted as evidence subject to the standard conditions mentioned earlier in the book.

For the second type (punishments), there is a well-known ikhtilaf. Abu Yusuf (and al-Jassas) hold that solitary reports can establish hudud, because they provide sufficient knowledge for action, just as evidence (bayyina) does in courts and just as textual indication (dalalat al-nass) does. Al-Karkhi disagrees, arguing that hudud are to be averted by doubts, and since solitary reports involve some doubt (as they are not mutawatir), they cannot establish hadd punishments, similar to qiyas. The passage then clarifies that bayyina (witness testimony) is based on a clear Quranic text (4:15) with no doubt, and notes that Abu Hanifa did not impose the hadd for liwat (sodomy) using qiyas or a solitary report (specifically a gharib report).

For the third type (pure obligations in servant rights, e.g., testimony for sighting the crescent of Eid), strict conditions are required: the wording of shahada (testimony), specified number of witnesses (if possible), proper guardianship competence, and all other conditions of reports. This is due to the heavy obligation and the risk of forgery.

For the fourth type (non-obligatory servant rights, e.g., agency, partnerships, gifts), solitary reports are accepted even without the reporter being 'adl (upright); only tamyiz (discretion) is required. Thus, the report of a child or a non-Muslim is accepted. The passage gives the example of a fasiq (corrupt person) informing someone of an agency appointment: if the listener's heart settles on his truthfulness, it is permissible to act on it. The text cuts off before listing the two reasons.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A solitary report transmitted by one or a few narrators, not reaching the level of tawatur.
بينة (bayyina): Clear evidence, typically witness testimony in court, established by a qat'i text.
عقوبات (ququbat): Divinely prescribed punishments (hudud) that are rights of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the acceptance of khabar al-wahid in matters involving pure obligation (third category)?
A) It is accepted with only the condition of discrimination (tamyiz), without 'adala.
B) It is not accepted at all because of the risk of forgery.
C) It is accepted only with the wording of shahada, proper number of witnesses, and all other conditions of reports.
D) It is accepted like the second category, with ikhtilaf among scholars.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of khabar al-wahid in Hanafi usul depends on the legal category: hudud are disputed, pure obligations require full testimony, and non-obligatory rights accept reports even from non-adl reporters.

The Four Types of 'Azimah Listening in Khabar Transmission
Chapter introducing fourth Sunnah division and its subdivisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of 'azimah listening for the listener in a khabar transmission and distinguish them from rukhsah.

Core Idea
Direct, unambiguous listening is 'azimah; indirect listening is merely rukhsah.

The fourth division of Sunnah, khabar, is not simply about any report; its evidentiary strength depends on how the report is received. The listener's side is divided into 'azimah (strict compliance) and rukhsah (concession). This lesson unpacks the four types of listening that qualify as 'azimah, distinguishing primary from successor types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions of the chapter
The chapter is split into two parts: one concerning the report itself, and one concerning something else (likely the conveyor). Focus on the report itself.
The second part is not detailed here.
2
Understand the two parties of the khabar
The report itself has two parties: the listener (sami') and the conveyor (muballigh). Each party has its own 'azimah and rukhsah.
This lesson focuses on the listener's side.
3
Differentiate 'azimah and rukhsah for the listener
'Azimah listening is clear, unambiguous hearing (islam' la shubhata fihi). Rukhsah is the absence of direct listening, e.g., reading a text without hearing the conveyor's voice.
'Azimah is stronger in evidentiary weight.
4
List the four types of 'azimah listening
The text states there are four divisions of 'azimah listening. Two are at the utmost degree of 'azimah, and two are their successors, resembling rukhsah.
The first two primary types: (a) conveyor reads to you from book/memory while you listen, (b) you read to him from book/memory while he listens and you verify.
5
Recognize the two primary and two successor types
The primary types are direct listening of the conveyor's recitation. The successor types are indirect but still considered 'azimah; they are not detailed in this passage.
Successor types have a shade of rukhsah because the listening is not entirely direct.

Source Text

Chapter: Explanation of the fourth division of the divisions of the Sunnah, which is the Khabar (report). This chapter has two subdivisions: a subdivision relating to the report itself, and a subdivision relating to... As for the report itself, it has two aspects: the aspect of the listener and the aspect of the conveyor. Each one of them has two divisions: 'azimah (strict compliance) and rukhsah (concession). As for the aspect which is the listener's aspect: the 'azimah in that is what is of the type of listening that has no ambiguity. And the rukhsah is what has no listening. As for the listening that is 'azimah, it has four divisions: two divisions at the utmost degree of 'azimah, and one of them has more right than the other. And two other divisions that take the place of the first two divisions; they are also from the category of 'azimah, but in the way of succession, so they have resemblance to rukhsah. And you listen (to it). As for the first two divisions: what he reads to you from a book or from memory while you (listen) and what you read to him from a book or from memory while he listens, and you say to him: Is it as...
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter explaining the fourth division of the Sunnah (al-qism al-rabi'), which is the Khabar (report). The author begins by stating that this chapter has two parts: one concerning the report itself, and one concerning something else that is not detailed here (likely the conveyor or the chain). He then focuses on the report itself, which has two parties: the listener (al-sami') and the conveyor (al-muballigh). Each of these parties is further divided into two categories: 'azimah and rukhsah. For the listener, 'azimah refers to clear, unambiguous listening (islam' la shubhata fihi), while rukhsah refers to cases where there is no direct listening (e.g., reading from a book without audial transmission). The author then enumerates the types of listening that fall under 'azimah: there are four types. Two are at the highest level of 'azimah, and one of these two is more entitled (i.e., stronger) than the other. The other two types are successors to the first two; they are still 'azimah but have a resemblance to rukhsah because they are indirect. The specific examples given: the first two types include when someone reads to you from a book or from memory while you listen, and when you read to him from a book or memory while he listens, followed by your confirmation: 'Is it as (you heard)?' The text cuts off here, but the pattern suggests further elaboration on the remaining types. This categorization is foundational for understanding how reports are accepted in Hanafi usul, emphasizing the importance of direct, unambiguous hearing for strict compliance.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar): The fourth division of Sunnah; it refers to reports (hadith) that are not mutawatir but are transmitted by one or more narrators.
العزيمة (al-'azimah): Strict compliance; the original ruling that requires direct, unambiguous listening without concession.
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): Concession; a lenient ruling where direct listening is absent, e.g., hearing from a book reading without audial transmission.
الإسماع (al-islam'): The act of making someone hear or listening; in this context, it refers to the audible reception of a report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three
D) One

Key Takeaway
The evidentiary strength of a khabar depends on the type of listening: direct, unambiguous listening ('azimah) is stronger than indirect listening (rukhsah), and 'azimah itself has four graded types.

Classification of Hadith Hearing: 'Azimah and Rukhsah
Classifies the two sides of khabar with 'azimah and rukhsah, detailing four types of hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of hearing in hadith transmission according to the Hanafi classification and explain why Abu Hanifa considers student recitation equivalent to teacher recitation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'azimah in hearing is fourfold; student recitation with confirmation is equally binding as teacher recitation.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces a crucial classification of how prophetic hadith are heard and transmitted. The author distinguishes between 'azimah (binding obligation) and rukhsah (concession) on the listener's side, and then breaks the binding obligation into four distinct forms. A key disagreement between the Hanafi school and the majority of hadith scholars arises over whether the student reciting to the teacher carries the same weight as the teacher's direct recitation. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating the authority of different transmission methods in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories: 'azimah and rukhsah
Recognize that the author first divides all modes of hearing into binding obligations and concessions. The binding obligation is further subcategorized.
2
Distinguish the two primary 'azimah modes
Note that these two are both in the highest degree of 'azimah, but one is more entitled (ahaqq) than the other according to the majority of hadith scholars. Abu Hanifa argues they are equal.
Language analysis: the word 'na'am' (yes) serves as a confirmation of approval.
3
Identify the two substitute 'azimah modes
These are also binding but by way of substitution (khilafah), resembling concession because the hearing is not direct.
The transmitter writes the hadith with chain and authorizes narration upon receipt.
4
Analyze the Hanafi-Ahl al-Hadith dispute
The majority consider teacher recitation superior because it mirrors the Prophet's method. Abu Hanifa counters that for fallible transmitters, both are equivalent, and student recitation is actually more cautious.
Key dalil: linguistic equivalence and the analogy with witnessing (shahada).
5
Apply the classification to a transmission scenario
Given a scenario where a student reads a hadith from a book and the teacher affirms it, the Hanafi school accepts it as sahih (sound) and binding, while other schools might require teacher recitation for highest authenticity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: Explanation of the fourth division of the divisions of the Sunnah, which is the report. This chapter has two divisions: a division that pertains to the report itself, and a division that pertains to the transmitter. As for the report itself, it has two sides: the side of the listener, and the side of the transmitter. Each of these is of two types: binding obligation and concession. As for the side that is the listener's side: the binding obligation in that is what is of the kind of hearing that has no doubt in it. And the concession is that in which there is no hearing. As for the hearing that is a binding obligation, it has four divisions: two divisions in the utmost degree of binding obligation, and one of them is more entitled than the other. And two other divisions that take the place of the first two divisions; they are also of the category of binding obligation, but by way of substitution, so they acquired a resemblance to concession. As for the first two divisions: what he recites to you from a book or from memory while you listen, and what you recite to him from a book or from memory while he hears, and you say to him: 'Is it as I recited to you?' and he says 'Yes.' The generality of the people of hadith said: The first division is of the two levels; indeed it is seen as the method of the Messenger (peace be upon him), and it is the absolute of hadith and oral delivery. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: That was more entitled from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) because he was protected from forgetfulness and he did not write, and our speech concerns one who is subject to forgetfulness, and he reads from a written text rather than from memory, and both are the same as oral delivery because the language does not distinguish between the statement of the speaker himself and that it be read to him, so he asks and says 'Yes'. Is it not seen that they are equal in performing testimony, even though the chapter of testimony is narrower than the chapter of hadith? And this is because 'Yes' is a word placed for repetition as an abbreviation, as previously mentioned, and the abbreviated is linguistically like the full; both are equal. And what we said is more precautionary because the attention of the student is stronger, customarily and naturally, so the one who reads is not trusted not to make a mistake, but the student is trusted in such a case. Your reliance on your own reading is stronger than your reliance on his reading. Only the possibility of his inadvertence regarding some of what you recited to him remains, and this is easier than missing something from the text or the chain, so much so that if the narration is from memory, then that aspect is more entitled, as you said. As for the other two aspects: one is the book, and the second is the letter. As for the book: it follows the format of books; he says in it: 'So-and-so narrated to us' until he mentions the text of the hadith. Then he says: 'When my book reaches you and you understand it, then narrate it from me with this chain.'
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the modes of transmitting hadith, specifically focusing on the listener's side. The author divides the types of hearing (isma') into 'azimah (binding obligation) and rukhsah (concession). The binding obligation hearing is further divided into four types: two primary and two substitute. The primary two: (1) the teacher recites from a book or memory while the student listens, and (2) the student recites to the teacher from a book or memory and asks for confirmation, to which the teacher replies 'yes'. The majority of hadith scholars consider the first type superior because it mirrors the Prophet's direct teaching. Abu Hanifa, however, argues that for fallible transmitters, both are equivalent because language does not distinguish between a person speaking directly and someone reading back to him with confirmation. In fact, Abu Hanifa considers the student's recitation more cautious because the student is more attentive. The other two substitute types are the written book (kitab) and the letter (risalah), where the transmitter writes the hadith with a chain and authorizes narration upon receipt and understanding. This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul for determining the validity of hadith transmission methods.

Key Terms
عزيمة (ʿazīmah): Binding obligation in transmission; the strict form of hearing that is free from doubt.
رخصة (rukḥṣah): Concession; transmission without actual hearing, e.g., ijazah.
مشافهة (mushāfahah): Oral delivery, face-to-face transmission of hadith.
الخبر (al-khabar): The report, i.e., the prophetic hadith being transmitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a mode of 'azimah hearing?
A) Teacher reciting from book while student listens
B) Student reciting from memory and asking teacher to confirm
C) Receiving a written book (kitabah) with a chain
D) Listening to a tape recording of a hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes four binding modes of hearing hadith, with student recitation and teacher recitation equally authoritative for fallible transmitters.

Distinction Between Haddathana and Akhbarana in Hadith Transmission
Defining terminological usage for direct vs indirect transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing 'haddathana' from 'akhbarana' to determine the appropriate transmission term and to assess oath-breaking in cases of written communication.

Core Idea
Haddathana is for direct oral transmission; akhbarana is for indirect—writing or messenger.

This lesson examines a foundational usul ruling from Al-Bazdawi's Usul that sets the correct terminology for hadith transmission. The distinction between direct oral hearing (mushafahah) and indirect receipt via writing or messenger is not merely terminological—it has legal consequences, particularly in the validity of oaths. The passage uses prophetic practice and Quranic evidence to establish this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that transmission can be direct (face-to-face) or indirect (via writing/messenger).
The text divides transmission into four categories: two direct and two indirect.
2
Extract the Hanafi rule
Note that the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is to use 'haddathana' for direct and 'akhbarana' for indirect.
This is based on linguistic usage and the practice of the salaf.
3
Examine the prophetic proofs
Review the hadith of Mu'adh, the delegation of Abdul Qais, and the commands to Abu Bakr, Umar, Bilal, and Zaid showing the Prophet used 'akhbara' for indirect conveyance.
These demonstrate that informing via messenger is 'akhbara'.
4
Analyze the oath ruling
Apply the rule to oaths: if one swears not to 'narrate' (haddatha) or 'speak' (takallama), writing does not break the oath; if swears not to 'inform' (akhbara), writing does break the oath.
This shows the legal consequence of the terminological distinction.
5
Connect to Quranic evidence
The verse 'wa kallama Allahu Musa taklima' (4:164) proves that 'taklim' (speaking) is specific to direct speech, reinforcing that 'haddathana' is for oral.
Allah informs through books (akhbara) but does not 'speak' directly except to Moses.

The foundation of religion. Likewise the letter/message is upon this basis. Do you not see that the conveyance of the Messenger, peace be upon him, was also by sending (1). (1) His statement: 'Do you not see that the conveyance of the Messenger... also by sending' includes: what the group narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, when he sent Mu'adh to Yemen said: 'You are going to a people from the People of the Book, so call them to the testimony that there is no deity but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah. If they obey you in that, inform them that Allah has obligated upon them five prayers every day and night. If they obey you in that, inform them that Allah has obligated upon them charity taken from their rich and given to their poor. If they obey you in that, beware of their valuable properties, and fear the supplication of the oppressed, for there is no veil between it and Allah.' And in the two Sahihs from Ibn Abbas that the delegation of Abdul Qais said to the Messenger of Allah: 'Command us with a decisive matter that we may inform those behind us and enter Paradise through it.' So he commanded them with four. The hadith. And from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, he said: The Messenger of Allah said to me: 'Go out and call among the people: Whoever testifies that there is no deity but Allah, Paradise is obligatory for him.' Narrated by Abu Ya'la. And from Umar ibn al-Khattab that the Messenger of Allah commanded him to announce among the people: 'Whoever testifies sincerely that there is no deity but Allah alone, without partner, will enter Paradise.' Narrated by Abu Ya'la and al-Bazzar. And from Bilal, he said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'O Bilal, call among the people: Whoever says there is no deity but Allah before his death by a year, a month, a week, a day, or an hour, will enter Paradise.' Narrated by al-Tabarani in al-Kabir. And in it from Zaid ibn Khalid al-Juhani, he said: 'The Messenger of Allah sent me to give glad tidings to the people that whoever testifies that there is no deity but Allah alone without partner, will enter Paradise.' And that is after they are established by proof. And the chosen view in the first two categories is that the listener should say: 'haddathana' because that is used for face-to-face speech. He said in al-Ziyadat: Regarding one who says: 'I spoke to so-and-so about such-and-such' or 'I narrated to him about it': it applies to face-to-face conversation. And in the other two categories, the chosen view is that he should say: 'akhbarana' because writing and sending are not face-to-face. Do you not see that we say: 'Allah informed us (akhbarana Allah), and told us (anba'ana), and gave us news (nabba'ana) through the Book and the message, and we do not say 'haddathana' or 'kallamana'? That is specific to Moses, peace be upon him, Allah said: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' (Quran 4:164). And for this reason we said regarding one who swears: 'He will not narrate this' or 'not speak about it': he does not break the oath by writing or sending a message, contrary to if he swears 'he will not inform about it': he breaks the oath by that.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Al-Bazdawi's usul on the proper terminology for hadith transmission. The main text establishes that 'haddathana' (حدثنا) is used for direct oral transmission (mushafahah) and 'akhbarana' (أخبرنا) for indirect transmission via writing or messengers. The commentary supports this with numerous hadith showing that the Prophet sent messages (رسالة) and used the term 'أخبر' for informing via letters. The ruling from Al-Ziyadat (زيادات) clarifies that if someone swears not to 'حدث' (narrate) or 'تكلم' (speak), writing or sending a letter does not break the oath because those actions are not considered oral speech. Conversely, if he swears not to 'أخبر' (inform), then writing or sending a letter does break the oath because 'إخبار' includes both oral and written communication. This shows that the Hanafi school distinguishes between direct and indirect transmission based on the linguistic usage of the salaf. The Quranic verse about Moses (4:164) is cited to show that 'تكليم' (speaking) is specific to direct speech from Allah, as opposed to indirect revelation.

Key Terms
حدثنا (haddathana): A term used for direct, oral, face-to-face transmission of a hadith or report.
أخبرنا (akhbarana): A term used for transmission via writing, letter, or indirect means, as opposed to direct oral encounter.
مشافهة (mushafahah): Direct, face-to-face verbal communication.
رسالة (risalah): A written message or letter sent to convey information.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person swears, 'By Allah, I will not speak (atakallamu) about this matter.' He writes a letter about it. According to the Hanafi ruling cited, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because writing is a form of speech.
B) No, because 'speaking' refers specifically to direct oral communication.
C) Yes, because he informed others by writing.
D) No, because oaths are only broken by verbal utterance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly distinguishes between direct oral transmission (haddathana) and indirect transmission via writing or messenger (akhbarana), based on linguistic usage and prophetic practice.

Conditions of Ijazah: Knowledge and Trustworthiness
Explaining the license (ijazah) and hand-delivery (munawalah) as alternative transmission methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid ijazah and apply them to determine whether a license to narrate is accepted.

Core Idea
Ijazah requires the recipient to know the book's content and be trustworthy in precision and understanding.

In the Hanafi school, not every license to narrate hadith is automatically valid. The text distinguishes between a recipient who knows the content and one who does not. This lesson unpacks the conditions for a valid ijazah and the recommended phrasing, showing how Hanafi usul protects the integrity of transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two transmission methods
The passage mentions ijazah and munawalah as forms of rukhsah. Both share the same condition for validity.
Rukhsah here means a concessional method without direct hearing.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios of the recipient
The recipient either knows the book's content (alim) or is ignorant (jahil). Only the knowledgeable scenario leads to valid ijazah.
3
Extract the conditions for validity
Two conditions: (1) recipient's knowledge of the content (alim), and (2) recipient's trustworthiness (ma'mun) in precision (dabt) and understanding (fahm).
The text explicitly states 'if the one receiving the license is trustworthy in precision and understanding'.
4
Recognize the recommended phrasing
The preferred expression is 'Ajaza li fulaan' (so-and-so gave me ijazah). Other expressions are also permissible, but the text is cut off.
The recommended phrasing ensures clarity in the chain.
5
Understand the rationale
Ijazah is a concession because it lacks direct hearing (isma'). Hence, stricter safeguards are needed to preserve the integrity of transmission.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijazah): A license granted by a scholar to a student to narrate specific works or his entire corpus, without requiring direct oral hearing.
مناولة (munawalah): Handing over a written copy of a hadith work to a student, often combined with an oral statement of permission.
رخصة (rukhsah): A concession or license; here, a method of transmission that is less stringent than direct hearing (sama').
مستجيز (mustajiz): The person receiving the license (ijazah); the student who is permitted to narrate.
ضبط (dabt): Precision or accuracy in memorization and transmission.
فهم (fahm): Understanding and comprehension of the transmitted material.

Key Takeaway
Ijazah in Hanafi usul is valid only when the recipient is knowledgeable of the transmitted content and is trustworthy in precision and understanding.

The Hanafi Distinction Between Haddathana and Akhbarana
Ruling on wording for oral vs written transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that 'haddathana' is reserved for oral transmission and 'akhbarana' for written transmission, and derive the ruling for oath-breaking cases.

Core Idea
Haddathana for speech, akhbarana for writing: the mode of transmission determines the term.

In hadith transmission, the choice between 'haddathana' (حدثنا) and 'akhbarana' (أخبرنا) is not arbitrary. The Hanafi school, following al-Bazdawi, establishes a clear distinction based on whether the communication was face-to-face or through writing. This distinction has practical effects not only in scholarly narration but also in everyday legal questions such as oaths. In this lesson, we will unpack the rule, its proof from the Quran, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of transmission
The passage divides hadith transmission into two pairs: the first two categories (oral) and the other two (written). Identify what distinguishes them.
Oral = direct speech; Written = letter/kitab.
2
State the rule for each category
For oral transmission, the listener should say 'haddathana'. For written, 'akhbarana'. Note that the rule is a preferred choice (mukhtar), not an absolute obligation.
The ruling is from al-Ziyadat, a Zahir al-Riwayah text.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The passage cites Quran 4:164, which uses 'kallama' for Allah's direct speech to Musa. This shows that 'haddathana' and 'kallama' are for direct speech, while 'akhbarana' is for indirect communication via Book.
The proof is Qur'anic, so transmission is qat'i, but the dalala (implication) is zanni.
4
Apply the rule to oath cases
If someone swears not to 'narrate' (yuhaddith) or 'speak' (yatakallam) something, writing does not violate the oath. But if they swear not to 'inform' (yukhbiro), writing does violate it because 'ikhbar' includes written communication.
This application shows the practical legal consequence.

And for this reason we said concerning one who swore: 'I will not narrate such-and-such' or 'speak it,' he does not break the oath by writing or letter; contrary to if he swore 'I will not inform such-and-such,' he does break the oath by that.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Bazdawi's usul on the appropriate terminology for transmitting hadith based on the mode of transmission. The author distinguishes two pairs: direct oral transmission (mushafaha) and indirect transmission via writing or letter. For the first two categories (direct), the listener should say 'haddathana' (he narrated to us) because this term is specifically used for face-to-face speech. For the other two categories (written), the listener should say 'akhbarana' (he informed us) because writing is not face-to-face. The author cites a proof from the Quran where Allah's direct speech to Musa is described using 'kallama' (spoke), while revelation via Book is described as 'akhbara' (informed). He then applies this distinction to oaths: if one swears not to 'narrate' or 'speak' something, writing does not break the oath; but if one swears not to 'inform' something, writing does break the oath because 'ikhbar' encompasses written communication. This ruling is from al-Ziyadat (a core book of Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms
حدثنا (haddathana): A term used by the listener in hadith transmission to indicate that the narrator directly spoke to him face-to-face.
مشافهة (mushafaha): Face-to-face oral communication between narrator and listener.
أخبرنا (akhbarana): A term used to indicate that information was conveyed, typically through writing or indirect means.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'haddathana' is reserved for oral face-to-face transmission, while 'akhbarana' is used for written or indirect transmission, a distinction derived from Quranic usage and applied to oath rulings.

Knowledge of Content: Condition for Valid Ijazah
Ruling on validity of ijazah with or without knowledge of content

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of knowledge for ijazah and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the validity of transmission without knowledge.

Core Idea
Ijazah without knowledge of content is invalid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

In hadith transmission, ijazah (license) allows a student to narrate a book without direct audition. But is it valid if the student does not know the contents? This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school. Understanding the condition of knowledge is crucial for determining whether a transmitted hadith qualifies as proof in religious matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the core question being debated? The passage focuses on whether an ijazah is valid when the recipient (licensee) does not know the contents of the transmitted book.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They hold that ijazah is invalid if the recipient is ignorant of the book's contents. Their proof is qiyas (analogy) to the judge's letter: testimony (ishhad) requires knowledge of the contents; similarly, transmission requires knowledge.
They analogize ijazah to the act of testifying about a letter, which requires knowledge of its content.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf allows ijazah even without knowledge in matters that are customarily kept secret. His qiyas is based on the custom of confidentiality, where knowledge is not required for permission to transmit secrets.
Abu Yusuf does not extend this to public documents; there he agrees with the others.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? If the ijazah is valid without knowledge, then a hadith transmitted via such an ijazah would be acceptable as proof. If invalid, the narration is not proof.
The author notes that the precautionary position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, aligning with the seriousness of the Sunnah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author considers the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad more precautionary (ahwat), implying it is the relied-upon view. He also suggests Abu Yusuf might have agreed in principle.
The mufta bihi is that ijazah requires knowledge of content for validity.

As for the license (rukhsah): it is that which has no audition, and it is ijazah (license) and munawalah (handing over). All of that is of two types: either the licensee is knowledgeable of what is in the book, or ignorant of it. If he is knowledgeable of it, and has examined it and understood its contents, and the licensor said: 'So-and-so narrated to us what is in this book according to your understanding through these chains, so I am by it from him and I have licensed you to narrate it,' then the ijazah is valid in this manner — if the licensee is trustworthy in precision and understanding. Then what is recommended in that is to say: 'So-and-so licensed me,' and it is permissible to say: 'haddathani' or 'akhbarani'. The better is to say: 'ajaza li', and it is permissible to say: 'akhbarani' because that is less than face-to-face. But if he does not know what is in it, the ijazah is invalid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, and it is valid based on the qiyas of Abu Yusuf's opinion, may Allah have mercy on him. The basis for this is in the judge's letter to a judge and messages: that knowledge of their contents is a condition for the validity of testimony (ishhad) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Abu Yusuf only permitted that in matters that are customarily kept secret, so he did not permit it in documents. Similarly, munawalah with ijazah is the same as mere ijazah; it is possible that it is not valid in this chapter, and possible that it is valid by necessity. It is only valid according to him if he is safe from increase and decrease. And the most precautionary is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them. It is also possible that Abu Yusuf's opinion is like theirs as well, because the sunnah is fundamental in religion, its matter is great, its affair is enormous. And validating ijazah without knowledge and understanding is lifting of trial, closure of the door of striving, and opening of the door of negligence and innovation. That is similar to the hearing of a child who is not qualified for reception — that is a thing for blessing, not a way by which proof is established. So too this. And as for one who sits in a listening gathering while he is occupied with something else, looking into another book...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the validity of ijazah (academic license) and munawalah (handing over of a book) in hadith transmission. The author defines ijazah as a form of transmission without audition (isma'). He presents two scenarios: the recipient knows the contents or does not. If the recipient knows the book's contents, has examined and understood it, and the licensor explicitly authorizes transmission, the ijazah is valid provided the recipient is reliable. The recommended phrase is 'ajaza li' but 'haddathani' or 'akhbarani' are also permissible, though less preferred because they imply direct audition. However, if the recipient does not know the contents, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad declare it invalid, while Abu Yusuf allows it by qiyas (analogy) to the case of a judge's letter to another judge, where knowledge of contents is a condition for testimony according to the former but not the latter. Abu Yusuf restricts this to confidential matters, not public documents. The author notes that munawalah with ijazah follows the same ruling. The precautionary position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because validating ijazah without knowledge could lead to negligence. The author compares it to a child's listening, which is only for blessing, not legal proof. The passage ends with a caution about attending a listening session while distracted.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijazah): An academic license authorizing a person to transmit a book or hadith without direct audition.
مناولة (munawalah): Handing over of a book physically, often combined with ijazah, considered equivalent to ijazah alone in this discussion.
إشهاد (ishhad): Testimony or witnessing; used here as analogy for condition of knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the valid transmission of hadith via ijazah requires the recipient to have thorough knowledge of the book's contents; ignorance invalidates it according to the majority view.
