Rejection of Khabar al-Wahid upon Narrator's Denial
Dispute on narrator denial invalidating khabar al-wahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on narrator denial and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the 'Ammar and 'Umar hadith.

Core Idea
A narrator's denial of his own report invalidates it in Hanafi usul.

This passage addresses a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: if a trustworthy narrator later denies or forgets his own narration, does his khabar al-wahid remain valid? The Hanafi madhhab argues that denial indicates a defect, analogous to testimony upon testimony. The opposing view, often associated with the Shafi'is, cites the incident of 'Ammar and 'Umar where a report was accepted despite 'Umar's forgetfulness. We will dissect the proofs and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the report is rejected because the narrator's denial overrides his earlier statement. Find where the text says 'ولأن النسيان محتمل... فلذلك بطلت بإنكارهم'.
The analogy to testimony upon testimony is key.
2
Analyze the opposing proof
Examine the hadith of 'Ammar and 'Umar. Understand how the opponent uses it to argue that forgetfulness does not invalidate a trustworthy narrator's report.
The opponent says 'Umar did not remember, yet accepted the hadith.
3
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Read the Hanafi rebuttal: the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain is not proof because the Prophet knew himself; the example of 'Umar shows he accepted based on trust, but that is not a general rule. Also, the possibility of forgetfulness cuts both ways.
The text states 'وحديث ذي اليدين ليس بحجة'.
4
Identify practical examples
Note the examples of Rabi'a and Suhayl, and the hadith of 'A'isha. These illustrate cases where narrator denial affects acceptance.
Understand how these examples support the Hanafi view.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a hypothetical: a reliable narrator reports a hadith on the requirement of wali in marriage, but later denies it. Using the Hanafi principle, would this hadith be accepted as evidence?
The answer is no; the denial invalidates it.

Source Text

ولأن النسيان محتمل من المروي عنه، بخلاف الشهادة على الشهادة ؛ لأنها لا تصح إلا بتحميل الأصول، فلذلك بطلت بإنكارهم.
والحجة للقول الثاني : ما روي عن عمار بن ياسر رضي الله عنه أنه قال لعمر رضي الله عنه : أما تذكر حيث كنا في إبل، فأجنبتُ، فتمعكتُ في التراب، فذكرت ذلك لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقال: أما كان يكفيك ضربتان» .
فلم يَذْكُرْه عمرُ، فلم يقبل خبره مع عدالته وفضله.
ولأنا قد بينا أن خبر الواحد يُرَدُّ بتكذيب العادة، فبتكذيب الراوي أولى.
وحديث ذي اليدين ليس بحجة ؛ لأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام ذَكَرَه، فعمل بذكره وَعِلْمه، وهو الظاهرُ من حاله، فما كان يُقِرُّ على الخطأ والحاكي يحتمل النسيان، بأن سمع غيره فنسيه، وهما في الاحتمال على السواء.
ومثال ذلك حديث ربيعة عن سهيل بن أبي صالح في الشاهد واليمين أن سهيلاً سئل عن رواية ربيعة عنه، فلم يعرفه، وكان يقول: حدثني ربيعةُ عني.
ومثل حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن وليها : فنكاحها باطل .
From them both [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]: Is it more correct what Dhu al-Yadain said? They said: Yes, so he accepted their testimony against himself regarding what he did not mention. And because forgetfulness is possible from the narrated person, unlike testimony upon testimony, for it is not valid except with the original witnesses being charged, therefore it becomes invalid by their denial. The proof for the second opinion: What is narrated from 'Ammar ibn Yasir (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said to 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): Do you not remember when we were among camels, I became junub and rolled in the dust, and I mentioned that to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he said: 'Would not two strikes have sufficed you?' So 'Umar did not remember it, yet his [i.e., 'Ammar's] report was not accepted despite his justice and virtue. And because we have already stated that khabar al-wahid is rejected by contradiction of custom, so by the narrator's contradiction it is even more appropriate. And the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain is not proof, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) mentioned it, so he acted based on his own mention and knowledge, and that was apparent from his state; he would not persist in error, and the narrator may have forgotten, by hearing from someone else and forgetting, and both are equal in probability. An example of that is the hadith of Rabi'a from Suhayl ibn Abi Salih concerning the witness and oath, that Suhayl was asked about Rabi'a's narration from him, and he did not recognize it, and he used to say: 'Rabi'a narrated to me from me.' And like the hadith of 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her guardian: her marriage is invalid.'
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage discusses a key usul dispute: If a trustworthy narrator (khabar al-wahid) denies his own narration (or forgets it), is his report accepted? The Hanafi position (presented first) is that the report is rejected because the narrator's denial (or forgetfulness) overrides his earlier statement. The proof given is analogical: just as testimony upon testimony is invalid if the original witnesses deny, because the chain is broken, similarly a hadith is invalid if the narrator denies, because forgetfulness is possible. The second opinion (likely the Shafi'i view) cites the incident of 'Ammar and 'Umar: 'Ammar mentioned a hadith to 'Umar, who did not remember it, yet 'Umar did not reject it outright but rather accepted it after 'Ammar's insistence. The Hanafi response argues that the hadith of Dhu al-Yadain (where the Prophet corrected his mistake based on testimony) is not proof because the Prophet himself knew and acted, and the narrator may have forgotten. Two examples are given: (1) the hadith of Rabi'a from Suhayl, where Suhayl did not remember narrating it, and (2) the hadith of 'A'isha about marriage guardian—implying that if the narrator denies, the hadith is not relied upon. The passage ultimately supports the Hanafi view that narrator denial invalidates the report.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators that does not reach the level of tawatur.
تكذيب الراوي (takdhib al-rawi): The narrator's own denial or contradiction of his earlier report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted because the narrator is trustworthy.
B) It is rejected because the narrator's denial indicates a defect, similar to testimony upon testimony.
C) It is considered mutawatir.
D) It is accepted only if corroborated by another narrator.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a narrator's denial invalidates his khabar al-wahid, based on the possibility of forgetfulness and analogy to testimony upon testimony.

The Rule of a Narrator's Contrary Action and Its Effect on Hadith Authority
Explaining when acting contrary to one's narration constitutes jarh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a narrator's action contrary to his narration constitutes jarh and apply the Hanafi rule that a hadith's proof status is invalidated only with certainty.

Core Idea
A narrator's contrary action only impugns his narration if it occurs after and with certainty, not before or doubtfully.

This lesson examines a foundational usul principle: when does a narrator's own practice contradicting his narrated hadith undermine the hadith's authority? Al-Bazdawi presents a nuanced rule distinguishing between actions before and after narration, and between certain contradiction and possible interpretation. Through concrete examples, you will learn to apply these conditions precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle and its three conditions
Read the passage and extract the core rule: a narrator's contrary action is jarh only if (1) it occurs after the narration, (2) it is a clear contradiction (khilaf bi yakeen), and (3) it is not based on a permissible interpretation of the hadith. Note also the exceptions: before narration, unknown date, or permissible interpretation do not constitute jarh.
The passage also states that if the date of the contrary action is unknown, the hadith remains a proof because doubt does not overturn certainty.
2
Analyze each example to see how the rule applies
Work through the examples given: (a) Hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn – Prophet acted on his own memory, narrator could forget, so not a proof. (b) Hadith of Suhayl/Rabe'ah – Suhayl did not remember his own narration. (c) Hadith of marriage without guardian – al-Zuhri did not recognize it, so not a proof for Abu Hanifa. (d) Internal Hanafi example: Abu Yusuf rejected Muhammad's narrations when Muhammad did not remember them. In each case, identify which condition (after/before, certainty/doubt) applies.
Note that in (c), the lack of recognition by al-Zuhri introduced uncertainty, so the hadith was not accepted as hujjah. This illustrates the requirement of certainty.
3
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a narrator who narrates a hadith about raising hands in prayer, then later is seen not raising his hands. Determine: (1) Did the contrary action occur before or after narration? (2) Is it a certain contradiction or could it be based on a different interpretation? State whether the hadith remains a proof according to the Hanafi rule.
If the action occurred after narration and is clearly contradictory (e.g., he explicitly says the hadith is false), it becomes jarh. If it occurred before, it is not. If he interprets the hadith differently (e.g., the hadith refers to a specific prayer), it is not jarh.

And the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn is not a proof because the Prophet, peace be upon him, remembered it, so he acted on his remembrance and knowledge, and that is the apparent state of affairs, so he would not persist in error, while the narrator may have forgotten, such that he heard from someone else and forgot it, and they are equal in probability. An example of that is the hadith of Rabe'ah from Suhayl ibn Abi Salih regarding the witness and the oath (1) that Suhayl was asked about Rabe'ah's narration from him, and he did not recognize it, and he used to say: 'Rabe'ah narrated to me from me.' And like the hadith of 'Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, who said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her guardian: her marriage is invalid' (3). That (1) Hadith of Rabe'ah: from Rabe'ah from Suhayl ibn Abi Salih from his father from Abu Hurayra who said: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, judged with the oath along with a single witness.' Reported by Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, and Abu Dawud, and added: 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Darawirdi said: I mentioned that to Suhayl, and he said: Rabe'ah informed me - and he is trustworthy with me - that I narrated it to him and I do not remember it. 'Abd al-'Aziz said: Suhayl had suffered an illness that removed some of his intellect, and he forgot some of his hadith, and Suhayl afterwards used to narrate it from Rabe'ah from him from his father. End. But it has other chains that are not sufficient for this, except that it is an example here, and Allah knows best. (2) Hadith of 'Aisha: from Sulayman ibn Musa from al-Zuhri from 'Urwa from that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her guardian: her marriage is invalid, her marriage is invalid, her marriage is invalid.' The hadith was reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah. Sulayman ibn Musa narrated it from al-Zuhri, and Ibn Jurayj asked al-Zuhri about this hadith, and he did not recognize it, so it was not established as a proof according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them. And an example of that is that Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, rejected some issues from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, which he (Muhammad) had narrated from him in al-Jami' al-Saghir, so he did not accept his testimony against himself when he did not remember it, and Muhammad confirmed that. As for if he acted contrary to it: if it was before his narration and before it reached him, it is not impugnment because the apparent is that he abandoned the hadith with a good opinion of it. And if he acted contrary to it after that in a way that is clearly contrary, then that is an impugnment in him because if that (the contrary action) is correct, then the proof with the hadith is invalidated, and if its opposite is false, then his narration is dropped by it, unless he acts according to some of what the hadith could mean, as we will explain if Allah wills. And if its date is not known, the proof with it is not dropped because it is a proof in origin, so it is not invalidated by doubt. And that is like the hadith of 'Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that the Prophet, peace be upon him... this... from al-Zuhri from 'Urwa from 'Aisha. Ibn Jurayj said: I met al-Zuhri and asked him about the hadith, and he did not recognize it. He said: Sulayman ibn Musa was such-and-such, and he praised him. And Ibn 'Adi reported it with the wording: Ibn Jurayj said: I met al-Zuhri and asked him, and he said: I fear that Sulayman may have been mistaken.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh rule regarding when a narrator's action contrary to his narration constitutes impugnment (jarh). The text opens by stating that the hadith of Dhu al-Yadayn is not a proof because the Prophet acted on his own memory, and the narrator could have forgotten—thus both possibilities exist, so certainty is not achieved. It then provides two examples: first, the hadith of Rabe'ah from Suhayl, where Suhayl did not remember his own narration, so it was not accepted. Second, the hadith of 'Aisha about marriage without a guardian; when al-Zuhri was questioned by Ibn Jurayj, he did not recognize it, so Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf did not accept it as a proof. The text also gives an internal Hanafi example: Abu Yusuf rejected some narrations of Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir because Muhammad himself did not remember them. The central rule is then stated explicitly: (1) If the narrator acted contrary before narrating or before learning the hadith, it is not jarh; (2) if after and with certainty of contradiction, it is jarh and the narration is dropped, unless the action is based on a plausible interpretation of the hadith; (3) if the date of the contrary action is unknown, the hadith remains authoritative because doubt does not overturn certainty. The passage ends by reiterating the example of 'Aisha's hadith with additional detail about al-Zuhri's concern that Sulayman may have erred. Throughout, the emphasis is on the necessity of certainty (yakeen) in establishing jarh, and the principle that a narrator's credibility remains intact unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
جرح (jarh): Impugnment of a narrator's reliability due to evidence of untrustworthiness, such as acting contrary to his narration with certainty
حجة (hujjah): A proof or evidence that establishes a ruling with certainty

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When it occurs before his narration
B) When it occurs after his narration but without certainty
C) When it occurs after his narration and is clearly contradictory with certainty
D) At any time, regardless of when it occurs

Key Takeaway
A narrator's contrary action only impugns his narration if it occurs after narrating and is a certain contradiction, protecting the hadith from being discarded due to doubt or interpretation.

Categories of Hadith Criticism from Non-Narrators
Classification of types of criticism affecting hadith from non-narrators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given criticism of a hadith from a non-narrator into its correct subtype and determine whether it is accepted or rejected based on the Hanafi usul criteria.

Core Idea
Criticism from a non-narrator is valid only if it is explained, based on a valid cause, and from a reliable critic.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a hierarchical classification for evaluating criticism (ta'n) that comes from someone other than the narrator of the hadith. Since such criticism does not involve the narrator's own chain, it must be rigorously assessed. The classification helps us understand which criticisms are considered valid in Hanafi usul and which are rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of criticism
Determine whether the critic is a Companion of the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wasallam) or a later imam of hadith. This first branch decides the basic framework.
Companions are generally reliable, but their criticism may still be conditional.
2
If Companion: check if the matter could have been hidden
If the criticized issue is of a type that could have been hidden from the Companion (e.g., a subtle legal point), the criticism may be accepted. If it could not have been hidden (e.g., an obvious practice), the criticism is rejected.
This distinguishes between matters that are public knowledge vs. those requiring specialized attention.
3
If later imam: check if criticism is vague or explained
If the criticism is vague (e.g., 'this hadith is weak' without reason), it is rejected. If it is explained with a specific cause (e.g., 'the narrator is a liar'), proceed.
Vague criticism is insufficient for rejecting a hadith.
4
If explained: check if the cause is valid for jarh
The cause must be one that is legally recognized as impugning a narrator's reliability (e.g., lying, innovation). If the cause is not valid (e.g., a minor mistake), the criticism is rejected.
Not every fault qualifies as a valid jarh.
5
If valid: check if the cause is ijtihadi or agreed upon
If the cause is subject to scholarly disagreement (ijtihadi), the criticism is weighed further. If it is unanimously agreed upon (muttafaq alayh), then proceed to check the critic's character.
Unanimous causes have more weight.
6
If agreed upon: check the critic's reliability
The critic himself must be described as precise (mutqin) and sincere (nasih). If he is known for bias or hostility, his criticism is not accepted even if the cause is valid.
This final step ensures the criticism is objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الطعن يلحق الحديث من قبل غير راويه وهذا على قسمين: ۱ قسم من ذلك : ما يلحقه من الطعنُ من قبل أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. ۲- وقسم منه : ما يلحقه من قبل أئمة الحديث. وما يلحقه من قبل الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فعلى وجهين: إما أن يكون من جنس ما يحتمل الخفاء عليه، أو لا يحتمله. والقسم الثاني على وجهين أيضاً: إما أن يقع الطعن مبهماً بلا تفسير، أو يكون مفسراً بسبب الجرح: فإن كان مفسراً : فعلى وجهين أيضاً : إما أن يكون السبب مما يصلح الجرح به، أو لا يصلح فإن صلح: فعلى وجهين أيضاً: إما أن يكون ذلك مجتهداً في كونه جرحاً، أو متفقاً عليه. فإن كان متفقاً عليه : فعلى وجهين أيضاً: إما أن يكون الطاعن موصوفاً بالإتقان والنصيحة، أو بالعصبية والعداوة.
Chapter: Criticism that Affects Hadith from Other than Its Narrator. This is of two types: 1. That which affects it from the Companions of the Prophet, peace be upon him. 2. That which affects it from the imams of hadith. What affects it from the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, is of two ways: either it is of the kind that could be hidden from them, or not. The second type is also of two ways: either the criticism is vague without explanation, or it is explained due to a cause of jarh. If it is explained, then also two ways: either the cause is such that it is valid for jarh, or not. If it is valid, then also two ways: either it is a matter of ijtihad regarding it being a jarh, or it is agreed upon. If it is agreed upon, then also two ways: either the critic is described as precise and sincere, or as biased and hostile.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter on criticism of hadith that comes from someone other than the narrator himself. It provides a detailed classification. The first major division is between criticism from the Companions and criticism from later hadith scholars. Within criticism from the Companions, it distinguishes between things that could be hidden from them (i.e., matters not well-known) and things that could not. The second major division (criticism from later imams) is further subdivided: if the criticism is vague, it is not accepted; if it is explained with a reason, then the reason must be valid for jarh, and if valid, whether it is a matter of ijtihad or unanimous. Finally, even if the cause is agreed upon, the critic's own reliability (precise/sincere vs. biased/hostile) matters. This hierarchical classification helps a usuli determine the weight of external criticism on a hadith.

Key Terms
الطعن (al-ta'n): Criticism or accusation that undermines the reliability of a hadith.
الجرح (al-jarh): A defect in a narrator that makes his narration rejected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a later imam provides an explained criticism with a valid cause that is agreed upon, but the imam is known to be biased, is the criticism accepted?
A) Yes, because the cause is agreed upon.
B) No, because the critic is biased and hostile.
C) Yes, because the criticism is explained.
D) It depends on the nature of the bias.

Key Takeaway
Criticism of a hadith from a non-narrator is only accepted after passing a hierarchical filter: explicit, valid cause, and an unbiased, reliable critic.

Types of Criticism on Hadith from Non-Narrators
Classifies criticism from Companions and imams with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the types of ṭaʿn (criticism) that can affect a hadith from Companions or imams, and explain why the Hanafi school attributes the apparent contradiction of 'Umar and 'Ali to naskh.

Core Idea
When a Companion’s practice contradicts a hadith, the Hanafi school presumes authenticity and invokes naskh, not rejection.

This passage from Imam al-Bazdawi's Usul (Kanz al-Wusul) systematically categorizes the kinds of criticism (ṭaʿn) that can affect a hadith from sources other than its own narrator—namely from the Companions (Ṣaḥābah) or from later hadith imams. The classification is binary and detailed, and it is immediately applied to a famous example: the hadith prescribing exile for unmarried fornicators, which both 'Umar and 'Ali opposed. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating how Hanafi usul reconciles apparent contradictions between hadith and Companion practice.

Guided Path
1
Trace the binary classification of ṭaʿn from non-narrators
The author divides ṭaʿn into two main sources: from Companions and from imams. For Companions, he further divides into whether the matter could have been hidden from them or not. For imams, he splits into explicit vs. vague, valid vs. invalid cause, ijtihādī vs. agreed-upon, and the criticizer's reliability. Follow each binary fork to build a mental tree.
Memorize the four levels of binary splits for the imam branch.
2
Identify the first example: the hadith of exile and Companion opposition
The author cites the hadith 'al-bikr bi al-bikr: jald mi'ah wa taghrīb 'ām' and notes that 'Umar swore never to exile again and 'Ali called exile a fitna. Determine why this is an instance of 'not conceivably hidden' (the first type from Companions).
The ḥadd punishments are public and well-known, so it is inconceivable that these senior Companions were unaware.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of naskh
Since the contradiction cannot be due to ignorance, the Hanafi solution is to attribute it to abrogation (naskh). The hadith is authentic, but the ruling of exile was later abrogated (perhaps by the practice or by another revelation known to the Companions). Understand why this preserves the integrity of both the hadith and the Companions.
This principle is a hallmark of Hanafi usul: Companion practice can indicate naskh of a solitary hadith.

Chapter on the defect that attaches to the hadith from other than its narrator. And this is of two types: 1. One type: that which attaches to it of criticism from the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him). 2. And a type: that which attaches to it from the imams of hadith. And that which attaches to it from the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) is in two ways: either it is of the kind that conceivably could have been hidden from them, or not. And the second type is also in two ways: Either the criticism is vague without explanation, or it is explained due to a cause of jarh. If it is explained, then in two ways as well: Either the cause is such that it makes jarh valid, or not. If it is valid, then in two ways as well: Either that (criticizer) is exercising ijtihad in considering it a jarh, or it is agreed upon. If it is agreed upon, then in two ways as well: Either the criticizer is described as possessing precision and sincerity, or partisanship and enmity.

As for the first type, an example of that is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "The virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for one year" (1). So 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) swore that he would never exile anyone ever (2). And 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: "Exile is sufficient as a trial" (3). And this is of the kind that cannot be hidden from them; because the establishment of hadd punishments is from the [؟] leaders [؟], and it is based on notoriety, and 'Umar and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) are from the guidance. So if it were authentic, it would not have been hidden. This is because we received the religion from them, so it is unlikely that it was hidden from them. Therefore, that (the apparent contradiction) is attributed to abrogation (naskh). Similarly, when 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) refrained from division (4) in the Sawad of Iraq:

(1) Chapter on the defect that attaches to the hadith from other than its narrator: the hadith of the virgin with the virgin from 'Ubadah ibn al-Samit said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: "Take from me, take from me, Allah has made a way for them: the virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for one year." Narrated by al-Tahawi. In Muslim's narration: "One hundred lashes and exile for a year." And in al-Bukhari's hadith of al-'Asif: "And upon your son: one hundred lashes and exile for a year."
(2) His statement: And 'Umar swore: al-Karkhi narrated in his Mukhtasar from Salim ibn 'Abd that 'Umar ibn al-Khattab flogged a man from Qays and exiled him to Sham, then the man apostatized from Islam and joined the Romans, so 'Umar when he heard said: "I will never exile anyone after him ever."
(3) His statement: And 'Ali said: al-Karkhi narrated in al-Mukhtasar from Ibrahim from him, al-Rahman, and there is a break in its chain.
(4) His statement: 'Umar refrained from division: Abu 'Ubayd narrated in Kitab al-Amwal, and Sa'id ibn Mansur in his Sunan from Ibrahim al-Taymi said: When the Muslims conquered the Sawad, they said =
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of criticism (ṭaʿn) that can affect a hadith from sources other than its own narrator—specifically from the Companions (Ṣaḥābah) or from later hadith imams. The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, categorizes these criticisms in a systematic, binary fashion. When criticism comes from a Companion, it is either a matter that could conceivably have been hidden from them (due to its obscurity) or not (due to its notoriety). Criticism from imams can be vague or explicit; if explicit, the cause must be valid for jarḥ (impairment of credibility); if valid, it may be based on ijtihād or consensus; and if based on consensus, the criticizer must be known for precision and sincerity rather than bias.

The text then provides a concrete example: the Prophet’s hadith prescribing exile (taghrīb) for unmarried fornicators (the virgin with the virgin: 100 lashes and one year exile). Both 'Umar and 'Ali are reported to have opposed or avoided the exile portion: 'Umar swore never to exile anyone again after a case where the exile led to apostasy, and 'Ali described exile as a trial. Since both 'Umar and 'Ali were among the foremost authorities and the hadd punishments are matters of public notoriety (shuhrah), it is inconceivable that this hadith was authentic yet unknown to them. Therefore, the apparent contradiction is explained by abrogation (naskh)—the exile portion may have been abrogated in their view. This is a classic Hanafi approach: when a Companion’s practice contradicts an apparent solitary hadith, the practice may indicate naskh or that the hadith was not widely transmitted.

Key Terms
الطعن (al-ṭaʿn): Criticism or defect that discredits a hadith or its narrator.
الجرح (al-jarḥ): Impairment of a narrator's credibility due to a specific cause (e.g., lying, weak memory).
الانتساخ (al-intisākh): Attributing an apparent contradiction to abrogation (naskh) of a ruling.
الشهرة (al-shuhrah): Notoriety or wide prevalence; a hadith that is well-known among the scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the contradiction between the hadith of exile and the practice of 'Umar and 'Ali not considered a rejection of the hadith?
A) Because 'Umar and 'Ali were known to be forgetful.
B) Because the ruling of exile is from the category of rulings that are well-known and not easily hidden, so their opposition suggests the ruling was abrogated.
C) Because the hadith is weak in its chain of transmission.
D) Because 'Umar and 'Ali never disagreed with any hadith.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul classifies criticism from non-narrators into precise categories, and when a Companion's well-known practice contradicts a hadith, the preferred resolution is naskh rather than rejection.

When Criticism Does Not Discredit: Three Invalid Grounds
Explaining what does not constitute valid discrediting of narrators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which criticisms of a narrator are considered invalid jarh and apply the Hanafi rule that general or vague criticism is not accepted.

Core Idea
Vague jarh is invalid; probity of narrators is presumed until proven with specific evidence.

In Hanafi usul, not every criticism of a narrator is accepted. The passage from Al-Bazdawi outlines three types of criticisms that do not constitute valid jarh: rare errors, general accusations, and vague references. Understanding these boundaries is essential for evaluating hadith reliability and preserving the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of invalid jarh
From the passage, list the three types of criticism that are not considered valid discrediting. Support each with the author's example.
The categories are: rare incidents, general/unspecified criticism, and criticism using tadlis or kinayah without clarification.
2
Examine the analogy with shahada (testimony)
The author compares jarh in hadith to discrediting a witness in court. Explain why the standard for rejecting a narrator is stricter than rejecting a witness.
In court, a verifier's absolute discrediting is not accepted; here it is even more stringent.
3
Apply the rule to the example of Abu Hanifa
Read the criticism that Abu Hanifa sent his son to copy his teacher's books. State why this does not constitute valid jarh according to the passage.
It shows his caution and reliance on written records for accuracy.
4
Distinguish valid from invalid use of kinayah
The passage explains that using a vague reference (kinayah) for a narrator is not automatically jarh. Determine when such usage becomes a valid discrediting.
Only if the speaker refuses to clarify when asked.

From him that he did not act upon the hadith of ablution for one who laughed loudly in prayer, and that was not a discrediting (jarh) because that is among rare incidents, so it could be hidden. As for criticism from hadith scholars, it is not accepted in a general sense because the probity of Muslims is apparent, especially in the early centuries. If rejection were obligatory by absolute criticism, the sunan would be invalidated. Do you not see that the testimony of arbitration is narrower than this, and absolute discrediting from a verifier is not accepted in it, so this is more appropriate. And if he interprets it with something that does not constitute discrediting, it is not accepted, such as the criticism of those who criticized Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that he sent his son to take the books of his teacher Hammad (may Allah be pleased with him). This indicates his mastery; because he would not permit narration except from memory and mastery, and he did not trust a strong memorizer even if he strove in his memorization and his control was good. So recourse to the teacher's books is a sign of his mastery, not a discrediting in it. And from that: their criticism by tadlis (concealing), that one says: 'So-and-so narrated to me from so-and-so' without connecting the hadith by saying 'he narrated to us' or 'he informed us'. They called it 'an'anah because it suggests the suspicion of irsal, but its reality is not a discrediting, as has passed, so its suspicion is more appropriate. They were on a journey, and Abu Musa led them in prayer. A one-eyed man fell into a well or something, and all the people laughed except Abu Musa and Al-Ahnaf, so he ordered them to repeat the prayer. And know that in what is mentioned there is a difficulty, for Abu Musa is the narrator of the hadith of ablution from loud laughter, as narrated by Al-Tabarani. And from that: their criticism by tamyiz (cloaking) against one who uses a kinayah for the narrator and does not name him or attribute him, such as the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri: 'Abu Sa'id narrated to me', which could be trustworthy or untrustworthy. And like the statement of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (may Allah have mercy on him): 'A trustworthy person from our companions narrated to us' without explanation, because kinayah about the narrator is not objectionable; it is for his protection from criticism, protection of the critic, and brevity. Not everyone who is accused in some respect loses all his hadith, like al-Kalbi and his likes, and like Sufyan al-Thawri despite his great rank and his advancement in knowledge and piety, and calling him trustworthy is a testimony to his probity, so how could it become a discrediting? The point of kinayah is that a man may be criticized falsely, so it is right to protect him, and he may narrate from someone younger than him, or his peer, or from his companions, and that is valid according to jurists and scholars of Sharia. If his chain is long, he uses kinayah to protect against false criticism. It only becomes a discrediting if he is asked for clarification and does not clarify.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage discusses the principles of jarh wa ta'dil (discrediting and verifying narrators) as applied in Hanafi usul. It opens by noting that rare incidents (like the hadith on laughing in prayer) do not constitute a discrediting of a narrator because they are exceptional. General, unspecified criticism from hadith scholars is not accepted because the default assumption is probity (adalah) of Muslims, especially early generations. The author draws an analogy with court testimony (shahada), where an unspecified discrediting (jarh mutlaq) from a verifier is not accepted; here it is even more fitting because accepting such criticism would invalidate many hadiths. Then it addresses specific invalid criticisms: (1) against Abu Hanifa for sending his son to copy his teacher's books – this actually shows care and mastery, not flaw. (2) Tadlis (using 'an'anah) is not inherently a discrediting because it may indicate careful transmission, though it creates suspicion of irsal. The example of Abu Musa is given: he rejected the action of laughing in prayer despite being the narrator of the hadith about ablution from laughter, which shows that his practice did not undermine his reliability. (3) Tamyiz (using kinayah or vague references) is also not a discrediting by itself, as it protects the narrator from false accusations and is a common practice among scholars. Such vagueness only becomes a discrediting if the critic refuses to clarify when asked. The passage emphasizes that great scholars like Sufyan al-Thawri cannot be discredited by such methods.

Key Terms
الجرح (al-jarh): Discrediting a narrator's reliability such that their narration is rejected.
التدليس (al-tadlis): Using ambiguous terms like 'from' instead of explicit 'heard from' in hadith transmission.
الكناية (al-kinayah): Referring to a narrator by a euphemism or description instead of naming directly.
التلبيس (al-talbis): Concealing the identity of a narrator or confusing matters intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the criticism against Abu Hanifa (sending his son to copy books) not a valid discrediting?
A) Because his son was a liar
B) Because it shows his caution and mastery in narration
C) Because Abu Hanifa never narrated from his son
D) Because the books were not authentic

Key Takeaway
General, unspecified criticism of a narrator is not accepted as valid jarh in Hanafi usul; specific and substantiated criticism is required.

Acceptance of Young Narrators in Hanafi Usul
A usul rule affirming that young age alone does not impugn a narrator's reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for accepting a young narrator's hadith and identify the example evidence used in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Youth does not disqualify a narrator; precision at hearing and uprightness at narration are what matter.

In Hanafi usul, the reliability of a narrator is not automatically tainted by young age at the time of hearing. The passage we will study clarifies the conditions that must be met and provides a concrete example from the Sunnah. This lesson will help you understand how the Hanafis approach jarh wa ta'dil with respect to age.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The passage states that criticism due to young age does not impugn. Restate this claim in your own words.
2
List the conditions
Find the three conditions mentioned: itqan at hearing, bulugh and adala at narration.
3
Locate the evidence
The passage gives an example: the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba. What does this hadith show? Why is it relevant?
4
Understand the dalala
Explain how the acceptance of this hadith by the ummah supports the rule that youth alone is not a defect.
5
Cross-reference with other schools
Consider how other schools might treat a young narrator and why the Hanafi position is unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك: الطعنُ بالصَّغَر، وذلك لا يقدحُ بعد أن يثبت الإتقان عند التحمل، والبلوغ والعدالة عند الرواية، مع ما تقدم ذكره. وذلك مثل حديث عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن صُعَيْر العُذري في صدقة الفطر أنها نصف صاع من حنطة.
And from that is criticism due to young age, and that does not impugn after it is established that there was precision at the time of hearing, and maturity and uprightness at the time of narration, along with what was previously mentioned. And that is like the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Su'ayr al-Udhri regarding zakat al-fitr that it is half a sa' of wheat.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that criticizing a narrator for being young (at the time of hearing hadith) is not a valid jarh, provided certain conditions are met: the narrator must have demonstrated precision (itqan) at the time of hearing, and must have reached maturity (bulugh) and be known for uprightness (adalah) at the time of narrating. The author cites as evidence the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Su'ayr al-Udhri, who narrated about zakat al-fitr being half a sa' of wheat. Abdullah was likely a young companion or narrator, yet his hadith is accepted. The passage underscores that young age alone, without evidence of incompetence, does not disqualify a narrator.

Key Terms
إتقان (itqān): Precision and accuracy in learning and preserving hadith
تحمل (taḥammul): The act of receiving or hearing hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The practice of Sufyan al-Thawri
B) The hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba about zakat al-fitr
C) The example of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan
D) A verse from the Quran

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a narrator's young age at the time of hearing does not disqualify him if he demonstrates precision (itqan) at hearing and uprightness (adalah) at narration.

Invalid Grounds for Criticizing Narrators: Non-sinful Actions and Youth
Explaining types of criticism that do not impugn a narrator's reliability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of criticism against hadith narrators are considered invalid in Hanafi usul and explain why, using the examples of non-sinful actions and youth.

Core Idea
Criticism of a narrator is only valid if it involves religious sin or precision defects; youth and non-sinful conduct do not disqualify.

In Hanafi usul, the reliability of hadith narrators is a cornerstone of legal evidence. However, not every criticism levelled against a narrator is acceptable. Al-Bazdawi explains that some forms of criticism—such as criticizing a narrator for actions that are not sinful in the Shari'ah, or for being young at the time of hearing—do not actually impugn their credibility. This lesson dissects these invalid grounds, using the example of Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan and the hadith of zakat al-fitr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of invalid criticism
From the passage, list the two broad categories of ta'n that do not disqualify a narrator: (1) criticism based on actions that are not sinful (e.g., manners, racing, joking), and (2) criticism based on youth (sighar).
2
Examine the example of non-sinful actions
Read the account of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani being criticized for his manners. Understand why the Hanafis reject this: jurists and ascetics have different standards; what is blameworthy for an ascetic may be praiseworthy for a jurist.
Also note the examples of horse racing and joking—both permissible in context.
3
Analyze the youth (sighar) argument
Understand that youth at the time of hearing does not disqualify if the narrator was precise (itqan) at the time of learning and upright when narrating. The passage uses the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba and Ibn Abbas as evidence.
The hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba (half sa' of wheat) is accepted despite his youth.
4
Apply the Hanafi tarjih principle
See how the Hanafis prefer the hadith of Ibn Abbas/Abdullah ibn Tha'laba over Abu Sa'id's hadith based on textual strength (thabata matnan), even though both are equally connected.
This shows that youth alone does not weaken a narration; other factors like textual stability can give precedence.
5
Derive the usul rule
Formulate the rule: Invalid criticism includes any attack that does not relate to religious integrity (diyanah) or precision (itqan). Non-sinful actions and youth are not valid grounds for rejection.
This rule governs how Hanafi scholars evaluate narrators.

ومن ذلك : ما لا يُعد ذنباً في الشريعة مثل طعن الجاهل في محمد ابن الحسن رحمه الله ؛ لأنه سأل عبد الله بن المبارك أن يقرأ عليه أحاديث سمعها ، فأبى، فقيل له في ذلك ، فقال : لا تعجبني أخلاقه. ولأن هذا إن صح فليس به بأس؛ لأن أخلاق الفقهاء تخالف أخلاق الزهّاد ؛ لأن هؤلاء أهل عُزلة، وأولئك أهل قدوة، وقد يحسن في منزل القدوة: ما يقبح في منزل العزلة، وينعكس ذلك مرةً. وقد قال فيه عبد الله بن المبارك : لا يزال في هذه الأمة مَن يُحيي به دينهم ودنياهم فقيل له : ومَن ذلك اليوم؟ فقال: محمد بن الحسن الكوفي. ومثال ذلك من طعن بركض الدابة مع أن ذلك من أسباب الجهاد، : كالسباق بالخيل، والأقدام. ومثل طَعْنِ بعضهم بالمزاح، وهو أمر ورد الشرع به بعد أن يكون حقاً، لا باطلاً، إلا أن يكون أمراً تستفزه الخفة، فيتخبط ولا يبالي. - ومن ذلك : الطعنُ بالصَّغَر ، وذلك لا يقدحُ بعد أن يثبت الإتقان عند التحمل، والبلوغ والعدالة عند الرواية، مع ما تقدم ذكره. وذلك مثل حديث عبد الله بن ثعلبة بن صُعَيْر العُذري في صدقة الفطر أنها نصف صاع من حنطة. ألا يُرى أن رواية ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما لصغره: لم تسقط، ولذلك قدمناه على حديث أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه في صدقة الفطر أنها صاعٌ من حنطة؛ لأنهما استويا في الاتصال، وهذا ثبت متناً. واختلف فيه على الزهري ففي رواية :مسدد عن ثعلبة بن عبد الله بن أبي صعير عن أبيه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. وفي رواية سليمان بن داود عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة أو ثعلبة بن عبد الله بن أبي صعير عن أبيه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. وفي رواية بكر بن وائل عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة أو ثعلبة بن عبد الله عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وهي رواية عبد الله بن يزيد عن همام عن بكر. عن عبد الله وفي رواية موسى بن إسماعيل عن همام عن بكر أن الزهري حدثه ابن ثعلبة بن صُعَير عن أبيه قال: خطبنا النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ورواه عبد الرزاق والطبراني والدارقطني والحاكم. حديث أبي سعيد : روى الجماعة عنه أنه قال: كنا نُخرج إذ كان فينا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم صاعاً من طعام، أو صاعاً من تمر، أو صاعاً من زبيب، أو صاعاً من أقط، فلم نَزَل كذلك حتى قدم معاوية المدينة فقال: إني لأرى مدين من سمراء الشام: تعدل صاعاً من تمر فأخذ الناس بذلك، قال أبو سعيد : فلا أزال أخرجه كما كنتُ أخرجه». لكن البخاري لم يذكر فيه : قال أبو سعيد وابن ماجه لم يذكر لفظ : أو في شيء منه وإنما قال: صاع من طعام صاع من تمر. قلنا : وهو أقرب إلى الصواب. وفي رواية أبي داود : «لا أُخرج أبداً إلا صاعاً». وأخرج الحاكم والطحاوي قال أبو سعيد وذكر عنده صدقة الفطر، فقال: لا =
Among that [which does not constitute valid criticism] is what is not considered a sin in the Shariah, such as the ignorant person's criticism of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, because he asked Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak to read to him hadiths he had heard, but he refused. He was asked about that, so he said: 'I do not like his manners.' And because if this is true, there is no harm in it, because the manners of jurists differ from the manners of ascetics. For these are people of seclusion, and those are people of leadership. What is bad in the station of seclusion may be good in the station of leadership, and the opposite sometimes occurs. And Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said about him: 'There will always be in this community someone through whom Allah revives their religion and their world.' He was asked: 'And who is that today?' He said: 'Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Kufi.' An example of that is criticism of urging on a riding beast, even though that is among the means of jihad, such as horse racing and foot racing. And like the criticism of some for joking, which is something the Shariah permits as long as it is truth, not falsehood, unless it is something that levity provokes, causing one to act recklessly without caring. And among that is criticism due to youth, which does not impugn after precision at the time of learning and reaching puberty and integrity at the time of narration, along with what was previously mentioned. That is like the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Su'ayr al-’Udhri regarding zakat al-fitr, that it is half a sa' of wheat. Do you not see that the narration of Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them, despite his youth, was not dropped? Therefore, we gave it precedence over the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding zakat al-fitr that it is a sa' of wheat, because both are equal in continuity, and this one is established as a text. There is disagreement about it from al-Zuhri. In the narration of Musaddad: from Tha'laba ibn Abdullah ibn Abi Su'ayr from his father from the Prophet, peace be upon him. In the narration of Sulayman ibn Dawud: from Abdullah ibn Tha'laba or Tha'laba ibn Abdullah ibn Abi Su'ayr from his father from the Prophet. In the narration of Bakr ibn Wa'il: from Abdullah ibn Tha'laba or Tha'laba ibn Abdullah from the Prophet—this is the narration of Abdullah ibn Yazid from Hammam from Bakr. In the narration of Musa ibn Isma’il from Hammam from Bakr that al-Zuhri narrated to him: Ibn Tha'laba ibn Su'ayr from his father said: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, addressed us.' And it was narrated by Abd al-Razzaq, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, and al-Hakim. The hadith of Abu Sa'id: The group narrated from him that he said: 'We used to give—when the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was among us—a sa' of food, or a sa' of dates, or a sa' of raisins, or a sa' of dried cottage cheese. We continued like that until Mu'awiyah came to Medina and said: 'I see that two mudds of Syrian wheat equal a sa' of dates.' So the people took that. Abu Sa'id said: 'I will continue to give as I used to give.' But al-Bukhari did not mention in it: 'Abu Sa'id said,' and Ibn Majah did not mention the word 'or' in any of it; he only said: 'a sa' of food, a sa' of dates.' We say: This is closer to correctness. In the narration of Abu Dawud: 'I will never give except a sa'.' And al-Hakim and al-Tahawi narrated that Abu Sa'id mentioned zakat al-fitr to him and said: 'No...'
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's usul discusses forms of criticism (ta'n) against narrators that do not actually discredit them. The first type is criticism based on actions that are not sinful in the Shariah. The example given is someone criticizing Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani (a major Hanafi jurist) because Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak refused to read hadith to him, saying he did not like his manners. The commentator explains that even if true, this is not a valid criticism because jurists and ascetics have different manners; what is blameworthy for an ascetic may be praiseworthy for a jurist who is a role model. In fact, Ibn al-Mubarak himself praised Muhammad ibn al-Hasan elsewhere. Another example is criticism for racing horses or urging on a beast, which is actually part of jihad. Similarly, joking is permissible as long as it is truthful and not excessive. The second type is criticism due to youth (sighar). Youth does not disqualify a narrator if he was precise at the time of learning and upright when narrating. The author cites the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba about zakat al-fitr (half sa' of wheat), showing that despite his youth, his narration is accepted. He also mentions the hadith of Ibn Abbas, who narrated despite being young, and his narration is preferred over that of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri regarding the measure of zakat al-fitr. The discussion on the various narrations of the hadith of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba and Abu Sa'id illustrates how scholars weigh conflicting narrations, preferring the one with stronger transmission or textual clarity. The Hanafi position is that the correct amount for zakat al-fitr is half a sa' of wheat based on the narration of Ibn Abbas and others, over the more common report of a full sa'.

Key Terms
طعن (ta'n): Criticism or disparagement of a narrator's reliability.
صغر (sighar): Young age at the time of hearing hadith; does not automatically disqualify if the narrator was precise and later upright.
قدح (qadah): To impugn or invalidate a narrator's credibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul as explained by Al-Bazdawi, which of the following is NOT a valid reason to impugn a narrator?
A) The narrator was known to lie about hadith.
B) The narrator was a young child when he heard hadith.
C) The narrator was accused of rafḍ (rejection of caliphs).
D) The narrator was found to be forgetful in his narrations.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, only criticism related to religious sin or precision flaws invalidates a narrator; youth and non-sinful actions are not valid grounds.

The Criterion for Narrator Acceptance: Itqan over Ihtiraf
Arguing that Ibn Abbas's hadith is preferred despite his youth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of 'al-ibrah bi sihhat al-itqan' (the criterion is validity of precision) to evaluate the acceptance of a narrator, using the example of Ibn Abbas's narration on sadaqat al-fitr.

Core Idea
Itqan (precision) determines narrator acceptance, not professional status.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi usul principle: the acceptance of a hadith depends on the narrator's precision (itqan) rather than his professional status as a hadith transmitter. The author illustrates this through the case of Ibn Abbas, whose narration on sadaqat al-fitr is preferred over that of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, despite Ibn Abbas's young age. By examining the application of this principle, you will learn to distinguish between formal occupation and substantive accuracy in hadith criticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul rule
Locate the central claim: the criterion for accepting a narration is precision (itqan), not professional practice (ihtiraf). This is stated directly: 'العبرة لصحة الإتقان'. The rule is placed in the context of a dispute about sadaqat al-fitr.
2
Examine the evidence for the rule
The author cites two proofs: (1) the Prophet's acceptance of the bedouin's report on moon sighting, and (2) the acceptance of Abu Bakr's narrations despite his lack of professional hadith training. Identify these in the text and note how they support the rule.
Both are examples of non-professional but reliable narrators.
3
Apply the rule to the specific example
Consider the hadith of Ibn Abbas on sadaqat al-fitr (two mudds of wheat or a sa' of barley/dates). The author argues that his narration is valid and even preferred over Abu Sa'id's, despite Ibn Abbas's youth, because it is equally continuous (muttasil) and its content is precise.
The rule overrules any objection based on youth or inexperience.
4
Reconcile the apparent conflict between narrations
The author reconciles the hadiths by interpreting 'food' in Abu Sa'id's narration as including wheat, barley, etc., and treats Ibn Abbas's 'two mudds of wheat' as an equivalent to a sa' of other staples. This shows how the usul rule operates in fiqh application.
The reconciliation is based on the principle that specific types are subsumed under the general term.

(١) حديث ابن عباس: أخرجه الحاكم وصححه، ولفظه عن ابن عباس أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر صارخاً يَصرخ في بطن مكة، يأمر بصدقة الفطر، ويقول: هي حق واجب على كل مسلم، ذكر أو أنثى ، صغير أو كبير، حر أو عبد، حاضر أو باد، مُدَّان من قمح، أو صاع من شعير، أو تمر. (٢) حديث الأعرابي في رؤية الهلال: تقدم في باب تفسير الشروط [ص٣٨٥].
Do you not see that the narration of Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, due to his young age, was not dropped? That is why we gave it precedence over the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding sadaqat al-fitr, that it is a sa' of wheat; because they are equal in continuity (of chain), and this is established in the text: 'a slave, male or female.' And there is disagreement about (the narration of) al-Zuhri: in the narration of Musaddad from Tha'laba ibn Abdullah ibn Abi Sa'ir from his father from the Prophet, peace be upon him. And in the narration of Sulayman ibn Dawud from Abdullah ibn Tha'laba or Tha'laba ibn Abdullah ibn Abi Sa'ir from his father from the Prophet, peace be upon him. And in the narration of Bakr ibn Wail from Abdullah ibn Tha'laba or Tha'laba ibn Abdullah from the Prophet, peace be upon him - this is the narration of Abdullah ibn Yazid from Hammam from Bakr from Abdullah. And in the narration of Musa ibn Ismail from Hammam from Bakr that al-Zuhri narrated to him (from) Ibn Tha'laba ibn Su'air from his father who said: The Prophet, peace be upon him, addressed us, and it was narrated by Abd al-Razzaq and from barley or... and al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, and al-Hakim. (1) Hadith of Abu Sa'id: The group narrated from him that he said: We used to give out when the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was among us, a sa' of food, or a sa' of dates, or a sa' of raisins, or a sa' of dried yogurt, and we continued like that until Mu'awiya came to Medina and said: I think that two mudds of Syrian wheat equal a sa' of dates, and the people followed that. Abu Sa'id said: I will continue to give out as I used to. But al-Bukhari did not mention in it that Abu Sa'id said, and Ibn Majah did not mention the word 'or' in any of it, but only said: a sa' of food, a sa' of dates. I say: That is closer to correctness. And in the narration of Abu Dawud: 'I will never give out except a sa'.' And al-Hakim and al-Tahawi narrated that Abu Sa'id, when sadaqat al-fitr was mentioned in his presence, said: No, from the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, and added to that is also the narration of Ibn Abbas. And from that (kind of criticism) is the objection that whoever did not make a profession of narrating hadith, his hadith is not valid; because the criterion is validity of precision. This is like the objection of those who objected to Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be pleased with him, that he did not make it a profession, even though those of lower status did, so likewise in every era, if precision is sound, the (lack of) practice is irrelevant. And the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, accepted the report of the bedouin about sighting the moon, and he was not accustomed to narrating. (He said:) 'I only give out what I used to give out in the time of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him: a sa' of dates or of barley.' A man said to him: 'Or two mudds of wheat?' He said: 'No, that is Mu'awiya's valuation, I do not accept that nor act upon it.' End. I say: This narration is similar to the other in closeness to correctness, for al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih, in the chapter of sadaqah before 'Eid, from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'We used to give out in the time of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, on the day of Fitr, a sa' of food.' Abu Sa'id said: 'Our food was barley, raisins, dried yogurt, and dates.' So it negates that 'food' is separate from barley and what follows. And by this the error of al-Hakim's narration becomes clear, which said 'a sa' of wheat' instead of 'food'. (1) Hadith of Ibn Abbas: Al-Hakim narrated it and authenticated it, and its wording is: 'Ibn Abbas reported that the Prophet, peace be upon him, ordered a crier to cry out in the valley of Mecca, commanding sadaqat al-fitr, saying: It is an obligatory right upon every Muslim, male or female, young or old, free or slave, present or absent: two mudds of wheat, or a sa' of barley, or dates.' (2) Hadith of the bedouin about sighting the moon: It has preceded in the chapter of explanation of conditions [p. 385].
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis and usul rule regarding the acceptance of hadith narrations. The author argues that the hadith of Ibn Abbas on sadaqat al-fitr (two mudds of wheat or a sa' of barley/dates) is given precedence over the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (a sa' of food) despite Ibn Abbas's young age, because both narrations are equally continuous in chain (muttasil). The key point is that a narrator's lack of professional training in hadith transmission (ihtiraf) does not invalidate his narration if he possesses precision (itqan). The author supports this with the example of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, who was not a professional narrator but whose reports are accepted, and with the Prophet's acceptance of a bedouin's report on moon sighting. The passage then examines textual variants in the narrations of sadaqat al-fitr, concluding that the correct amount is one sa' of food (ta'am) as per Abu Sa'id, not specifically wheat. The Hanafi position here is that Ibn Abbas's hadith is stronger due to its chain despite his youth, and that the criterion for acceptance is reliability, not professional status. This is a fundamental usul principle: 'al-ibrah bi sihhat al-itqan' (the criterion is validity of precision).

Key Terms
محترف (muhtarif): One who makes hadith narration his profession or habit.
إتقان (itqan): Precision and accuracy in transmission; the true criterion for accepting a narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary criterion for accepting a hadith from a narrator?
A) The narrator being a professional hadith scholar
B) The narrator's precision (itqan) and reliability
C) The narrator's age and experience
D) The narrator's frequent practice of narrating

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the acceptance of a narration depends on the narrator's precision (itqan), not on professional status (ihtiraf), as exemplified by the Prophetic acceptance of the bedouin's report and the preference of Ibn Abbas's hadith despite his youth.

The Concept of Ta'arud and Its Resolution: The Case of Donkey's Su'r
Textual analysis of a usul example on conflicting evidences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for valid ta'arud (conflict of evidences) and apply the Hanafi method to resolve apparent conflicts by evaluating authenticity and chronology.

Core Idea
True ta'arud requires equal strength; a weak or later hadith cannot create a genuine conflict.

This lesson examines a concrete example from Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the conflicting hadiths about the meat of domestic donkeys and the implications for the purity of their leftover water (su'r). The commentator resolves the apparent contradiction by applying the strict definition of ta'arud, showing that not every opposite ruling constitutes a valid conflict. Understanding this analysis will sharpen your ability to weigh evidences in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting hadiths and their content
First, read the passage to extract the two hadiths: one prohibiting donkey meat (narrated by Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa) and one permitting it (narrated by Ghalib ibn Abjar). Also note the companion opinions on the leftover water.
The hadiths are ahad, so they are zanni in transmission and meaning.
2
Assess the authenticity of each hadith
The commentary states that the prohibition hadith is authentic (sahih) because it is in Bukhari and Ahmad, while the permission hadith is weak (mudtarib) because its chain is disturbed. Determine whether both are equally strong.
Mudtarib is a type of weak hadith due to irreconcilable contradictions in the chain.
3
Check chronology
The permission hadith mentions that it was narrated after the prohibition, as it refers to a drought situation after Khaybar. The later hadith would normally abrogate the earlier, but since it is weak, it cannot abrogate a stronger proof.
In Hanafi usul, a weak hadith cannot abrogate a stronger one.
4
Apply the Hanafi definition of ta'arud
Recall that ta'arud requires equality of strength, unity of time and place, and that both proofs are applicable to the same subject. Here, the hadiths are not equal in authenticity, and the time may differ.
The passage explicitly states the conditions: 'تقابل الحجتين على السواء، لا مزية لإحداهما على الأخرى'.
5
Conclude the resolution
Since the prohibition hadith is authentic and the permission hadith is weak and later, no valid ta'arud exists. The authentic prohibition prevails, and the ruling on su'r follows accordingly (it is disliked, but the detailed ruling is beyond this lesson).
The example shows how to resolve apparent contradictions without abandoning either proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال القسم الثاني من القسم الرابع: سؤر الحمار والبغل؛ لأن الدلائل لما تعارضت) ، ولم يصلح القياس شاهداً؛ لأنه لا يصلح لنصب (۱) باب المعارضة : قوله : لما تعارضت الدلائل : قال الشارح: فإن عبد الله بن أبي أوفى روى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حرم لحوم الحمر الأهلية يوم خيبر. وروى غالب بن أبجر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أباح لحوم الحمر الأهلية. كان يكره التوضؤ بسؤر الحمار والبغل وابن عمر وابن عباس يقول : سوره طاهر لا بأس به. قلت : حديث عبد الله بن أبي أوفى رواه البخاري في صحيحه ، والإمام أ. مسنده»، وحديث غالب بن أبجر رواه أبو داود. وأثر ابن عمر رواه ابن أبي شيبة. وأثر ابن عباس لم أقف عليه. ولقائل أن يقول : لا تعارض في هذا فقد قلتم : إن المعارضة : تقابل الحجتين على السواء، لا مزية لإحداهما على الأخرى في حكمين... إلى آخره، بشرط اتحاد الوقت والمحل... إلى آخره. وليس شيء من هذا موجوداً فيما نحن فيه، فحديث التحريم صحيح، وحديث الإباحة مضطرب، فلم يوجد ركن المعارضة. ولم وحديث ابن أبجر متأخر، ففي لفظه : قلت يارسول الله ! أصابتنا السنة، يكن في مالي ما أطعم أهلي إلا سمان ،حُمُر ، وإنك حرمت لحوم الحمر الأهلية؟ وو
And an example of the second type of the fourth division: the leftover water of the donkey and mule, because when the evidences conflict, and analogy is not valid as a witness because it is not suitable for establishing [a ruling]. (1) Section of Conflict: His saying: When evidences conflict, the commentator said: Indeed Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa narrated that the Prophet (PBUH) prohibited the meat of domestic donkeys on the day of Khaybar. And Ghalib ibn Abjar narrated that the Prophet (PBUH) permitted the meat of domestic donkeys. He [the commentator] used to dislike performing ablution with the leftover of donkey and mule, and Ibn Umar and Ibn Abbas say: His leftover is pure, no problem with it. I say: The hadith of Abdullah ibn Abi Awfa is recorded by al-Bukhari in his Sahih and by Imam Ahmad in his Musnad, and the hadith of Ghalib ibn Abjar is recorded by Abu Dawud. The athar of Ibn Umar is recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba. The athar of Ibn Abbas I have not found. One might say: There is no conflict in this, for you have said: Conflict is the mutual opposition of two proofs equally, with no advantage of one over the other in two rulings... etc., with the condition of unity of time and place... etc. And nothing of this exists in what we are dealing with, for the hadith of prohibition is authentic, and the hadith of permission is weak (mudtarib), so the pillar of conflict is not established. And the hadith of Ibn Abjar is later, for in its wording: I said, O Messenger of God! The drought afflicted us, and I had nothing in my wealth to feed my family except fattened donkeys, and you have prohibited the meat of domestic donkeys?
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis within the usul discussion of conflicting evidences. It provides a concrete example: the legal status of the leftover water (su'r) of domestic donkeys and mules. The apparent conflict is between a hadith prohibiting donkey meat and a hadith permitting it, with companions' opinions on the leftover water. The commentator (al-Sharh) resolves the conflict by examining the authenticity and chronology: the prohibition hadith is authentic (sahih), while the permission hadith is weak (mudtarib) and also later in time. Therefore, no true ta'arud exists because the conditions for conflict—equal strength, same time, same subject—are not met. The prohibition thus takes precedence. This demonstrates the usul principle that for a valid conflict, proofs must be of equal weight; otherwise, the stronger prevails.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The leftover water or drink after an animal has drunk from it; its ruling affects ablution and purity.
تعارض (ta'arud): The mutual opposition of two evidence proofs of equal strength such that they cannot both be applied simultaneously on the same subject.
مضطرب (mudtarib): A weak hadith due to irreconcilable differences in the chain of narration, making its authenticity doubtful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator in this lesson, why is there no real conflict (ta'arud) between the hadiths of prohibition and permission regarding donkey meat?
A) Both hadiths are weak and thus cannot conflict
B) The prohibition hadith is authentic and the permission hadith is weak and later
C) The hadiths were revealed at different times but are equal in strength
D) Conflict only occurs in matters of purity, not meat

Key Takeaway
Valid ta'arud requires equal strength; when one proof is stronger, the weaker cannot create a genuine conflict.
